THE SMEW COMPANY OF AUSTRALIA
LIMITED.
Managing' Director : JOHN HOWELL (late General
Manager of the Broken Hill Proprietary Co., Limited).
ADDRESS:
Head Office—s 6 Margaret-st., Sydney.
THE Works are now in course of construction at Lake
Illawarra, N.S.W., and a portion will be in opera-
tion shortly, when the Company will be prepared to
purchase and treat, in large or small quantities, all classes
of Gold, Silver, Lead, Copper, and other Ores.
An ASSAY OFFICE and LABORATORY have been
opened at the Head Office in Sydney, and most com-
modious Assay Offices and Laboratories are now in
course of erection at the works, which will be equipped
in the most Modern Style, at both of which establish-
ments samples of ores of all classes will be tested for
the guidance of those not familiar with the value of
same.
Treatment of base refractory ores of low grade a
speciality.

THE RED PAGE.
Alice, and I, and Memory.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We supped together in modest state—
Alice, and I, and Memory.
We supped together : the hour was late t
“He will not mind—for once ! ” she said.
And I smiled, and she, and nodded her head.
And the silver shafts in her brown hair gleamed,
And my face was sad with the grief of Time;
Yet, “Do you remember . . . that night . . •
we dreamed ? ”
Her eyes flashed Memory over the years,
And I looked, and saw ... “0 Alice, not
tears ! ’’
So I filled her glaBS, and bade her quaff—
But she, merrily : “No, till your lips touch !”
And I touched, and she, with her old-time laugh.
And Memory sat on the glasses’ brink
And oh! so tenderly watched us drink.
And I was a youth, and she was a maid :
Our eyes were bright, and we locked warm
hands,
And gazed at the future unafraid . . .
And midnight was chiming! . . . Ah Alice, ah
me!
How old, how sad, was Memory !
A Book of the Girlish Imagination.
Lilian Turner joins sister Ethel in the great
company of bookmakers. Her first-published
long story, “The Lights of Sydney, or, No Past
is Dead,” gained first prize in a competition
organised by an English firm of publishers, and
was issued last year as a magazine Christmas num-
ber. It now attacks the public more formally in
a volume (posted from Bulletin office, 3s. and 45.).
The book is an excellent beginning. Miss
Turner’s literary style is fluent, and her matter
has fair emotional interest. She should become a
very popular writer. And it is popularity that
pays.
“The Lights of Syduey ” takes credit for one
thing : it is not merely a collection of chapter
episodes tagged on to the characters after “ Pick
wick Papers ” fashion : there is a definite attemp i
at plot and continuity and climax. But the plot
has the ludicrous twang of the Young Ladies
Journal. Miss Turner is all right when she gets
her characters on to the working plane, but all
beginning and ending the strain of lifting-up anc
taking-down is tremendous.
The main hinge is Auntie’s legacy. If Ida’s boy
marries Cousin Frew’s eldest girl, he gets £30,000;
and another £30,000 is divided between Ida and
Cousin Frew. If either party refuses to marry, the
.£60,000 goes to the other party. Auntie didn’t
provide for the contingency of both refusing to
marry, and whacking the £60,000. Auntie never
does : she puts her trust in the Machine-God, and
He inevitably pulls her through.
Enter Ida’s Wioked Husband. He is labelled
“Very Bad Man,” and he trims his nails with a
pen-knife before his wife, and has “many other
unpleasant habits.” What he has done beyond
trimming his nails with a pen-knife isn’t clear;
for Ida won’t tell. It isn’t infidelity, or cruelty,
or desertion ; but he is a Very Bad Man. So Ida
deserts him, as is the prescriptive right of woman,
and takes her two-year-old son along with her.
This son is called Jem.
The Wicked Husband is in Melbourne: Ida
flees to Sydney. And what does the Wicked
Husband do? Does he pursue her, and bring back
the precious key to the £60,000 ? Not at all. He
knows there is no difficulty nowadays in-personat-
ing the real Sir Roger. So he buys from his
sister her two-year-old boy, his nephew, for
“thousands,” brings him home to the suburban
nursery, and calls him James. Of course, two-
year-old babies are as much alike as pins, or peas,
or drops of water ; and nobody suspects nothing.
It will be the easiest thing in the world to prove
the bona-fide James an impostor—and his mother.
“ Ha ! ha ! ” chuckles the Very Bad Man.
This news is at onoe brought Ida by a trusted
friend—-a Very Good Man, who doesn’t compre-
hend the true inwardness of it. And what does
Ida do ? Does she indignantly denounce the vil-
lainy which aims to defraud her son of his rights,
and make him a—what? Not at all; she ac-
quiesces calmly; tells the trusted friend nothing
of the truth. The Machine-God will have it so.
And the Machine-God repays her. How ?
Why, by good luck Cousin Frew up-the-country
wants a governess, and sure enough the M.G. gets
Ida the billet. “ Widows ” with a boy two years
old are always popular as governesses, and stay a
long time in their situations. Ida stays about 17
years; and all that time her identity is unsus-
pected, and James grows up with Cousin Frew’s
eldest girl, Catherine, just as Auntie would like
it, and is treated as one of the family, as gove
nesses’children are always treated, and Mrs. Fre
is a sister to the governess. And the Very Bac
Man comes on a visit with James, and meets h:
son Jem, and hears about Ida, and goes avva-
happy and ignorant. The Machine-God has
sweat a bit just here.
But now Miss Turner is into deep water, anc
can swim. She has all the concessions she want
can fall to describing Barbara. Barbara
Catherine’s younger sister, and makes up th
quartette.. And Barbara is good : almost the on
character in the book who is more than a clothe
horse for incidents and sentiments. She is
anchor of hope in the trouble that Miss Turne
may become a mere writer of women’s books fo
women.
But there must be no tangle about Jem anc
James. (Miss Turner tangles them herself, anc
there is a lightning change of characters ’twix
Jem and James towards the close.) The situatio
at this stage is—
Jem, the manly real Sir Roger, loves madcap Barbara.
James, the quiet Claimant, also loves madcap Barbara.
Barbara is going to love James (alter the transformation
Catherine, the quiet heiress, loves Jem.
There is obviously room for a little complicate
love-play here, with the parents pulling strings fo
Auntie, and Miss Turner manages her oppor
tunities capably. And from a side-entrance com
a sister of Claimant James, and her dead baby
which Barbara picks up at Katoomba, givin
opportunity for Harradenish reflections of whic
some are fair and some tolerable and some in
tolerable. And by this time the pages are movin
past 200, and the Day of. the Machine*Lord is a
hand.
Suddenly Jem, the real Sir Roger, loses his bole
enchanting attributes, and becomes a fit mate fo
the meek Catherine, that the will of Auntie ma;
be fulfilled. And James, the quiet Claimant
puts on the cloak of manliness which drops frou
the retiring Jem, and bustles Barbara into marry
ing him. They even change names—Jem become
J ames, and James J em. It is a fearful thing for
a hero to fall into the hands of the Machine-God.
Then all the characters are called before the
curtain. The Very Good Man threatens the
Very Bad Man with gaol, and the V.B.M. Bays il
he goes the conniving Ida will have to go too
This aspect of affairs so overwhelms the company
that the V.B.M. walks off with a substantia
share of Auntie’s plunder, and is Wicked ever
afterwards, no doubt. The Machine-God oughi
really to have run a tram-car over the Very Bac
Man.
Sufficient has been said to show how Miss Tur-
ner stands from the point of view of Literature.
In construction, her book is orude and clumsy
but, given the characters and incidents, she
handles her matter well, sometimes very well. It
is all foreshadowed in an illustration on page 9.
You see Ida deserting her husband, with her two-
year-old on one arm and her bag on the other. It
is a pretty picture, and if you will look at it merely
as a pretty picture, and not hamper it with ques-
tions of probability and reality, it has some in-
terest and possibly charm. But it is not in the
least like a woman deserting her husband in real
life. Ida in the picture is coming down the
steps quite peacefully with her gloves on,
accurately buttoned, and just the right amount of
cuff and shoe showing. Her bag is a very small
bag, into which she might get one frock and a
comb ; but there is certainly no room for baby’s
clothes. And baby sits quite nicely and smiles :
he does not wriggle and twist, and his weight
does not give Ida the least inconvenience, though
she is holding him all on one side to make a nice
picture. And Ida has no parasol, and no cloak,
and her dress sits beautifully, and although she
lived at least 25 years ago it is cut in almost the
latest fashion, with the familiar puffed sleeves.
And there are no fluster or excitement, or
injunctions to the two-year-old to sit still,
or wild grabs at the railings, or untied laces,
or agitated recollections whether she has put
everything in. It is all “ pretty,” and “artistic,”
and three-fourths of it absolutely untrue. So
with “ The Lights of Sydney.”
But the public—the great public of the Young
Ladies’ Journal —doesn’t want truth in litera-
ture.
Rev. Fitchett’s rechauffe of British nava
and military history in “Deeds that Won the
Empire ” is well enough made, but any capable
journalist could have made it as well. Eitchett’s
merit comes in his fine eye for a popular topic,
with great (journalistic) results attainable by little
labor.
Geo. Robertson and Co. issue a new
edition of G. H. Gibson’s “Ironbark Chips and
Stockwhip Cracks.” The numerous illustrations
include some of Percy Spence’s best work in
black and white. Some of Mr. Gibson’s verse
first appeared in The Bulletin, and his book has
previously been noted as readable. His ballad-
models seem to have been taken from Tom Hood
and Gilbert, and he dates unmistalzeably in show-
ing not a trace of Kipling’s influence. Posted
from this office, Is. lOd.
A Poetical Sex-Flower.
ars^ (Music Professor at
University) issues some SCO lines “To
Irene m the metre and possibly after the model

to suggest the fashion in which the bard em-

poetieal fashion ; but most of the suggestions of

little poetical thought. So Keats may rest easy,
prettily k est r^ymes prettily—even very
Thus their two hearts were as one trembling lyre
With passion of wild music all afire,
It was with them as when some island tide
Feels against his the faint limbs of his bride,
And all his being quivering beneath
The timorous pant and pausing of her breath,
And her voluptuous summer-bosom’s swell
Warm outlined on his own, and all the sped
Of beauty maddening in his passionate blood
Till on her warm wild lips his glowing flood
Is broken in one breathless, deathless kiss,
And all her being surrenders unto his,
And earth and sea together fus4d lie,
Glowing in one, beneath one glowing sky.
The air hereabouts seems warmer than it
usually is in the groves of Academe; and
voluptuous summer-bosom’s swell ” may be
turned three times on the palate—resolutely sup-
pressing suggestions of (1) Byron’s “ voluptuous
swell, (2) summer-boarders, (3) a dude strutting
with a clean dickey. At his worst M.-L. is mixed
or stiff, or troubled with awkward or 111-soundint
decasyllabics like—
Or through grey miser's hour-glass time's sparse grains.
And past a myriad half-uttered sounds of things.
Fulfilling there almighty love's almighty heats.
Leaning upon his breast her glorious head,
And he comforting her was comforted.
For the rest, there are “large curled lips,” anc
“soft amorous cheeks,” and “swift impetuous
tresses ” —the whole box of fleshly phrases, in
fact, or as many of them as Mrs. G. will stand
(is there any Mrs. M.-H.?), And careful perusal
is sufficiently rewarded by—
Thus in the fair night shone the fairer moon,
And in the moon the mere, and in the mere
A little isle, set like a queenly crown
Upon a foaming cataract of hair.
And—
Fine sounds, like harp-tones through high chanoels rolled,
Piercing the heart with rapturous despair:
And—
He felt all flush on his her melting faoe,
Upon his ardent eyes her hungry gaze.
And through her glowing vest, the short sharp shocks of
bliss
That maddened in her bosom, maddening his,
And those empurpled winy grapes her mouth,
Crushed in his lips' insatiable drouth;
And all their limbs all faint with fond desire,
And filled with life’s infatuate fever-smart,
That loosens the deep fountains of the heart,
Infesting every vein with streams of fire.
Notes.
“ Infesting ” seems queer; but is correct: the
true English meaning borrows the Latin metonymy
—“troubling,” “plaguing.” Curious how the
su ggestion of number has crept into contemporary
meaning - through association with bands of
thieves “infesting” a district? For nowadays
the suggestion of plaguing is dominated by that
of pervading : “My place is infested with rats”
doesn’t necessarily contain any hint of worry.
And in Longfellow’s verse—
And the night shall be filled with musio,
And the cares that infest the day
Shall fold their tents like the Arabs,
And as silently steal away—
“infest,” for most people, means simply “fill.”
There is still a bad taste in the word, but it seems
going. Already, perhaps, you might venture
“The poem is infested with beauties” without
causing the general reader much discomfort.—No,
Dr. Murray’s big dictionary isn’t up to “I” yet.
“Short sharp shock.”— That slight mental hesi-
tancy comes from W. S. Gilbert—
To sit in solemn silence in a dull, dark dock,
In a pestilential prison with a life-long lock,
Awaiting the sensation of a short, sharp shook,
From a cheap and chippy ohopper on a big, black block 1
What a nuisance memory is !
“Mouth” and “drouth”—of course. They
were made for each other. When Le Gallienne
kisses “just thar,” he remarks—
O love 1 love 1 I fear,
For the air is too sweet—
Softly., love—O my dear—
Yea, too sweet
On a sudden to meet
Mortal mouth !
O my sweet I O my sweet!
The long drouth!
And at last, dear,
O think—
The long drink l
But “mouth” is a lovely word in any case—the
melting m and the soft breath of the final th are
things to dote over. You do not get the fragrance
in Byron’s—
That, womankind had but one rosy mouth,
To kiss Ultra all at once from north to south ;
But it comes in Kendall’s “ September ”
Sweet month, with a mouth that is made of a rose.
English melody, indeed, owes much to the soft th
“ Out of the sweet south sliding,” “The vasty
halls of death,” and so on.
Reflections.
But it was already decided that learning kills
the imagination.
Well, well, suppose humanity had stayed
asexual, like the earthworms, or hermaphrodite,
like oysters at Is. a plate ?
Chas. Lyne’s Life of Parkes is to be pub-
lished in a few weeks at sixteen shillings. Sixteen
shillings is stiff : is Parkes worth sixteen shillings ?
Certes, the volume comes with a testimonial from
its subject— “full,” “accurate,” “ honest,”
“painstaking,” the old man calls it. Yet are
these exactly the epithets one would wish applied
to a magnum opus ? (And Parkes measured
his epithets—he did.) The Life of Parkes
shouldn’t be too monumental, and respectable,
and solid, and dull. It should gambol a little (he
did). And it might misplace an aspirate occa-
sionally (he did) —but Mr. Lyne has seen to this
already : vide Chap. XLY.—“ An Heroic Strug-
gle.”
And if Mr. Lyne wants some irregular
lines to light np a blank page, here are those
which G. K. Maclean has sent to The Bulletin.
They are far too enthusiastically phrased for The
Bulletin, and the key of the whole, the Baconian
cipher-word “IminOrtal,” has the last of the
three vowels incorrect. But the biographer will
not mind that.
Slipshod work, ” ‘ ‘ absolute want of ori-
ginality,” “general level of dullness,” “weari-
some to the flesh,” are some of Lond. Spectator ’s
comments on A. F. Calvert’s “Exploration of
Australia ’’—apparently written for the lucky
speculator by some literary hack.
The first issue of ‘ ‘ The Australian Chess
Annual” (Geo. Robertson and Co.) does credit to
Editor Bignold and his contributors. (With at-
tractive modesty, the editor heads a list of
“ Chess Notabilities ” with his own not-too-
notable name. It may be alphabetical, but it
is not judicious.) The book reviews chess history
in all Australasian provinces, records the games of
the Esling-Wallace championship match, gives an
appendix of problems, and another on the Char-
lick counter-gambit, and supplies other miscella-
neous information. Posted from this office, Is. 3d.
F. : “ Soap and water for Sydney Lending
Library habitues.” They supply ’em in Melb.
Library, and there have been several prosecutions
for stealing the soap. But isn’t the soap dodge a
mere mousetrap for a rabbit plague ? The unclean
derelicts who haunt public libraries are, many of
them, walking menageries of bacteria, visible and
invisible. In Melb. Library one occasionally
opens a book to find obscene man-parasites be-
tween the pages. Insectiferous loafers should be
treated as the semi-criminals, semi-invalids
that they are. Attorney - General Isaacs
some time since took off his coat in th&
Melb. Library the more conveniently to ransack
law-books. He was officially forced to resume
his coat, and a press correspondence ensued. The
undisclosed reason behind the official act was that
if Isaacs were allowed to take off his coat in the
law gallery, the dirty fellows in the fiction bay
downstairs would want to take off their ooats,
with results too gross to specify. Some of the
favorite Melb. volumes could be fried in their
own fat. One theory for missing books is that
they walk away, like proverbial cheese. Can’t
insectiferous loafers be kept out of our libraries ? ”
HENRY PARKES.
In the dim past, great men have lived and died
They had their gaudy day ; but Time, alas !
Hath blotted out their glory and their pride:
Their deeds and virtues were as brittle glass I
Nay, more, their very names are set aside.
But this Immortal whom we now lament
Hath writ his large renown, with pen of fire,
Around the Half-Globe where he pitch’d his tent.
Though rear’d a hireling, he did high aspire ;
And men made way as for a demi-god.
He was their Idol: they were but his tools ;
Nor did he hesitate to break each rod,
For, in his mind, all men were knaves or fools:
A wily, suasive, caustic, studious soul;
Whose strong imperious nature clutch’d control.

Medical.
COUGHS, COLDS ON THE CHEST, BRON-
CHITIS AND ASTHMA RAPIDLY
CURED BY Hearne’s Bronchitis (Jure.
Those who have taken this medicine are amazed
at its wonderful influence. Sufferers from any
form of bronchitis, distressing cough, difficulty of
breathing, hoarseness, pain or soreness in the
chest, experience delightful and immediate relief,
and to those who are subject to colds on the chest
it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.
“Mr. W. G. HEARNE. —Dear Sir, —Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in
succession I had to sit up. I write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first, dose of
the medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily
improved as I continued the treatment, until I be atne,
as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely, H. WALKER,

The Shadow of the Bars.
[The following verses are from the pen of Thomas Suf-
field, now under sentence of 10 years penal servitude in
N.S.W. for an alleged criminal assault of which, in this
paper’s opinion, he was never proved guilty. Wensley-
dale, it appears, is Suffield’s English birthplace.]
(The shadow of the bars upon
The cell-wall in the moonlight :
The shadows of the trees upon
The moonlit laxvn at home.)
Oh ! Wensleydale ! dear Wensleydale !
What tho : I be so far away,
And lonely pace a prison cell \
To hold a deadly wrong at bay ;
The moon she crosses, white and pure,
My narrow prison’s barred pale':
And she shall be a silver lure
To lead me back—to Wensleydale.
Pall! bolt and bar ! Ring ! steel and stone !
At fall of night, and break of day,
This body is your lien alone ;
This heart unfettered flies away,
By glow and glt-am, thro’ cloud and rain,
O’er wild and waste, o’er hill and vale,
O’er steep and strand, o’er sea and main,
My heart is back in Wensleydale.
Oh ! Wensleydale ! dear Wensleydale !
Not bolt nor bar can part us two ;
Though cherished hopes lie deathly pale,
I still have left the past with you !
Once more, upon the wooded lawn,
I watch the flickering shadows fail;
Once more, upon the river borne,
I hear the bells of Wensleydale !
Once more, and thro’ this wicket gate
The well-known path be mine to-night,
Where the great beeches meet and mate,
Grim sentinels in black and white.
Here —resting on this uncouth seat—
I once was told the fondest tale !
Do Earth and Heaven ever meet ?
I thought so once—in Wensleydale.
’Tis Summer, and the thirsty mere
Hath deeply drained old Tore, I know,
For, falling over Mickley Weir,
His song is breaking faint and low.
Hack Fall’s far thunder trembles down;
Bass-droning thro’ the listening vale,
The rock-stemmed river’s monotone,
The deep keynote of Wensleydale.
Hark ! perched on Mowbray’s ivied height,
A throstle, startled at the moon,
Wakes, half in fear, and half delight,
And lifteth such a wondrous tune !
His mate, deep ambush’d in the dene,
Takes up and fills the liquid tale ;
And sprites of silver, gray, and green
Chant echo all thro’ Wensleydale.
H nr sh o’er the softer sounds of night
O d 'Ebor bays (the great blood-hound);
lucre starting, moonlit, dimly white,
The old home guards its hallowed ground !
With twinkling lights the casements glow ;
Familiar voices faint and fail,
And music soft and laughter low
Thrill “ Home, sweet home ! ” in Wenslet"Hl< 1
Pure lilt of love ! soft stray of bell !
Ah ! plaint of weir, and broken fall !
Sweet sounds, sweet prayers, and —sweetest
spell—
The hallowed homeliness of all.
Surely the night-wind, soft and shy,
Confessing all the sinless vale.
Must breathe a shrift in every sigh
“ Hush, Wensleydale!” “Hush Wensleydale! : ’
THOMAS SUFFIELD.
The N.S.W. Baptist Union has once more
emphasised its dislike of strong drink, and for-
warded a resolution to that effect to both Houses
of Parliament. 80011 after a type-written stanza
from an old writer was freely circulated among
the brethren :
A Bendigo (Vic.) lady seems to have go< d
cause to complain of the weakness of the alleged
strong arm of the law. Some little time ago a
man named Huggins called on her and walloped
her severely, and the law rose up after the event
and gave him seven days. He served his term,
and the very day he came out he called on the
lady and walloped her a great deal harder than
the first time. The law wasn’t around to interfere,
but when the episode was all over it arrived and
gave Huggins four months. His victim is now
trying to get her health up so as to stand another
attack from Huggins when he comes out. Judging
from his persistence, and the way the law never
arrives in time, she reckons that he is going to
spend the rest of his life in gaol for assault, and
whenever he comes out he is going to whack her
again, and then go back to gaol. In fact, she
looks forward to being intermittently whacked by
Huggins for the remainder of her days.
A miracle of love divine
Changed all the water into wine :
Save us from miracles of men
Who want to change it back again

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which "you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.

to be addressed'to William Macleod, Managing Director.

Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
1 Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding tioo columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture ivhich has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
ivhich such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C. ; Gordon
and Gotch, Ludgatc Circus ; Street A Co:, Cornhill.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 21, 1896.
The Scramble for the Capital.
One day last week G. Reid, the “jolly and
fat” Premier of N.S. Wales, gave another
shove, in his own characteristic fashion, to
the cause of Federation. At that moment
Australia was still waiting with what patience
it might, for N.S. Wales, the chronic stum-
bling-block, to move out of the road. The
holding of the Federal Convention was in its
usual state of indefinite postponement pend-
ing the time when the Reid Government
would condescend to say when it would be
sufficiently rested to think further over the
matter. As the Federal Constitution hasn’t
yet been drafted, and the Convention which
is to draft it hasn’t met, and the delegates
who are to form the Convention haven’t even
been elected, and no one knows when they
will be elected because Premier Reid refuses
so far to say anything definite about the
matter, and as no one can tell when he will
say anything definite, the future prospects
of the movement are about as shadowy as
they could possibly be. Nevertheless, Pre-
mier Reid last week urged the Assembly to
consent to the erection in Sydney of a palatial
and enormously-expensive House of Parlia-
ment, explaining in an off-hand manner that
it was proposed to make it especially large
and gorgeous in order that it might hold the
Legislature of the future Commonwealth.
Si dney, he added, would be the capital of
Federated Australia, therefore, of course,
the necessity of the new structure was quite
apparent. Then the matter was referred to
the Public Works Committee, which is now
considering the advisability of building in
advance a £470,000 house for the unborn
Legislature of the Federation which has not
yet arrived, and which, if it does arrive,
will choose its own capital and its own
dwelling-place.
* * * *
This infantile folly of the portly jester
whom New South Wales has made the head
of’ its Government is only one item in the
long list of that province’s sins against the
cause of Australian Unity. From almost
any other person than George Reid, the
insolent assumption that Sydney must neces-
sarily be the centre and capital pf the
Commonwealth would have gone a long way
towards breaking up the Federal movement
altogether, and, though Premier Reid
is now the specially - licensed buffoon
of Australian politics, even his attitude
counts for something in the matter. Mr.
Reid has gone out of his v r ay to emphasise,
for the dozenth time or thereabouts, that
New South Wales is in the Federal move-
ment only for what she can get. That pro-
vince has been all along the principal obstacle
to union, and, at the same time, the one
which demanded most emphatically that, of
whatever immediate cash-profit there might
be in the movement, she must have the
largest share. When a first humble be-
ginning was made by the establishment of
the Federal Council at Hobart, the mother-
province was the only one of the six Aus-
tralias which consistently refused to take any
part in the proceedings. A great deal of the
weakness and purposelessness of that Council
was probably owing to this defection; if it had
represented all Australia it might possibly have
grown to be something better than it was, but
as matters stood it merely petrified. When the
famous Convention of ’9l took place New
South Wales condescended to be represented.
One of its representatives— Parkes, the then
Premier—started the spirit of brotherhood
by reciting a mass of garbled statistics re the
greatness of New South Wales and the small-
ness and poverty of her principal rival,
Victoria ; and another—his successor, Dibbs
—truculently demanded that the very first
business should be to recognise Sydney as
the capital of the proposed Union. Even in
those early days the N.S. Wales representa-
tives stated in effect that their province was
in the movement only for plunder, and that
the honor and profit of possessing the seat of
Government was the price of its adhesion.
* * * *
Since then its attitude has been varied and
uncertain. It was N.S. Wales which blocked
the movement for three years by refusing
even to introduce the Convention Bill as
promised in the ’9l Convention. There are
three cities which demand loudly and per-
sistently to be nominated as the Federal
capital, and all three (Sydney, Bathurst, and
Albury) are in N.S. Wales. The present
Bathurst Convention, though its principal
unavowed purpose is to secure the nomina-
tion of a certain obscure trader to the Legis-
lative Council, is also called largely for the
purpose of advertising the demands of the
City of the Plains to be the future seat of
government. One dreadful politician even
advanced it as one of the special advantages
of Bathurst by comparison with Albury that
a Federal Legislature sitting there would not
be so liable to fall under “Victorian in-
fluences.” And this statement pretty well
represents the attitude of the cornstalk pro-
vince in the matter. The politician in question
blandly assumed that the capital should be in
N.S. Wales, asa hiatterof course; that Victoria
shouldn’t be allowed any influence whatever
in the Legislature—the idea of Queensland,
S.A., Westralia or Tasmania even expecting
to have any influence was presumably so im-
possible that they were not worth mention-
ing ; that Federation is to consist in New
South Wales governing the rest of Australia
as a species of conquered dependency ; that
the Federal Legislature will naturally have
no voice in fixing the seat of government ;
and that Sydney will naturally run the whole
show. And Premier Reid, who announces
that Sydney will be the Federal centre—he
being apparently the Federal movement in
his own person and having selected the
metropolis on his own responsibility—and
proposes to build a palace to accommodate
the future Australian Legislature, goes even
one better than the Bathurst convention or
the local fathers of little, obscure, sleepy
Albury.
• * * *
If there is ever going to be anything in
the alleged great Federal cause, there will have
to be a very serious climb-down on the part
of the so-called mother province. Except
for a little obscure wail from Tasmania,
which apologetically submitted its dreary
town of Hobart as a possible metropolis, all
the other provinces have advanced no special
claims of their own. None of them has
demanded possession of the centre of
Government as a matter of right, or urged
that the Legislature of the Common-
wealth should be kept clear of the
“influences” of the other provinces, or
insisted—as some members of the dominant
political party in Sydney have done—that
the other provinces shall accept their special
fiscal gospel as an indispensable condition of
union. And if Federation is going to be a
permanent success the bumptious cornstalk
person must consent to be an ordinary mem-
ber of the political family, and take his
chance with the rest. The selection of the
metropolis or of the place to create a metro-
polis must be made by the representatives of
all Australia, and if they do their duty they
will set aside all mere provincial ambi-
tions and consider solely the interests of the
continent at large. The future capital should
be as central as possible, safe from foreign
invasion, and selected with a view to the
future when population will be diffused
throughout all the habitable parts of Aus-
tralia—not to the casual and accidental pre-
sent, when, owing to purely transient circum-
stances, it is mainly fringed along the
eastern coast. If the choice lies among two
or three sites, the one chosen should, other
things being equal, be that where population
is thinnest; the establishment of the Federal
capital will create population, and the object
should be to draw it where the country most
wants development. In any case, Sydney
and Melbourne should both be left abso-
lutely out of consideration; they already
contain much too large a proportion of this
country’s population for the country's good,
and Australia is practically a big continent
which lives to maintain two bloated cities,
therefore, it is bad policy to artificially bloat
either of them any further. Also, Sydney,
Melbourne, Adelaide, Hobart, Perth, and
Brisbane should all be struck off the list of
possible candidates as being exposed and not
easily defensible. But whatever site is
selected, the bumptious N.S.W. politician
has no claim to any special consideration,
and the sooner he realises it the better.
* * • *
Federation means striking an average
among six possibilities. If the future Aus-
tralian Legislature does its duty conscien-
tiously, without fear or favor, the dominant
party in New South Wales may, or may not,
find a rigid protective tariff fixed upon it,
just as the majority of votes chances to go.
It may secure the centre of Government, or,
on the other hand, that coveted distinction
may go to South Australia, or Queensland,
or even Victoria, and Sydney be left a mere
provincial city, and Albury and Bathurst be
passed by without a semblance of considera-
tion. New South Wales may supply the
largest half of the Federal Ministry, or, again,
its politicians may prove very small and
ordinary individuals when they appear in a
larger arena, and the first Federal Cabinet
may not contain a single N.S.W. representa-
tive, and the province which claimed the
metropolis as a matter of right may find it-
self practically bossed by some dreadful Tas-
manian person running a legislature at some
place like Thargomindah or Hergott. Also,
Federation may bring a vast stream of com-
merce to Port Jackson, or, on the other
hand, the abolition of border tariffs, and the
expansion of the railway system under
Federal management, and the abolition of
differential rates may take away commerce
especially from the remote parts of the
province, into new channels. That Federa-
tion will do much good to Australia at
large is certain ; but if New South Wales
is only going into it on the understanding
that it gets all the principal advantages, and
has a special, immediate boom all to itself,
then it hasn’t begun to understand the
meaning of the term.
* * * *
The principal reason put forward by Syd-
ney politicians why their province should
have a certain undefined supremacy and be
duly or unduly grovelled to in this matter is
that N.S. Wales is the oldest member of the
Australian group. But against this there is
the fact that it is no older geographically
than any other, and that its people
are no older than other Australians,
and that their fathers weren’t any older
than the fathers of other Australians,
and that if their city is older than
other Australian cities it wasn’t the present
generation which made it older, therefore the
present generation can claim no special con-
sideration on that account. Moreover, there
is no visible reason why the capital should be
in the oldest province any more than in the
youngest one, or the middle one, or the one
half-way between the middle and some other
place. It is also claimed that New South
Wales has the most extensive population—
and these are about all the reasons advanced
for its large, aggressive attitude. Against
th 5 m may be set the equally relevant fact
that Westralia is the largest province, and
that Tasmania is the smallest, and that Vic-
toria is the most thickly peopled and best
cultivated, and has the largest city and the
most historical bank smash ; and that
Queensland has for many years shown the
largest percentage of population increase,
and the biggest debt per head ; and that
South Australia has the fewest criminals and
illegitimate births and the longest unpayable
railway—all of which are equally valid proofs
that any or every one of them should claim the
capital also. Any one set of proofs is about
as sound and about as ridiculous as the other.
The moral of it all is a very old one, but one
that can hardly be too often repeated. The
object of Federation is to boom all Aus-
tralia ; not to help any one province or any
one set of small provincial politicians at the
expense of the rest. And the policy of New
South Wales in scrambling for the capital of
the Commonwealth before there is any
Commonwealth, and proposing to build a
home for the Legislature which doesn’t yet
exist, and arrogating to herself a position of
leadership to which she hasn’t been appointed
and which she has done nothing to earn, is
the best way to break up the Federal move-
ment altogether.
The Q.N. Bank “Revelations.”
The Queensland National Bank is now an-
nounced to be utterly insolvent, and the usual
wail for State assistance goes up to heaven and
to Premier Hugh Nelson —the representative on
earth of the only heaven which theQ.N.B. just
now cares for. That the Queensland National
would cast itself on the State when private
management brought it down to the last pos-
sible stage of insolvency and muddle and mis-
representation, went without saying. The
theory of Australian banking in good times is
that the State can’t run a bank; that for it to
even propose to do so is ridiculous—this with a
smile of lofty contempt from the private bank-
ing “ expert ; ” that State banking would lead
to corruption and immorality ; that it is itself
immoral; also, that it is impossible, ruinous,
and a mere dream of the ignorant, brutal Labor
party or of the anarchist who elocutes by
Yarra’s muddy shore. And in bad times, when
the bank is cronk, and the cooked balance-sheet
can’t pass muster any longer, the same lofty
expert declares it to be the special business of
the State to take over the responsibility, and
put things straight, and run the bank till they
are put straight, and then hand it back in good
order and condition. And when the State has
finished doing this the private “expert” will
doubtless say as confidently as ever that the
State can’t do banking business, and that pri-
vate management is the only successful kind in
existence. It is a noble sight when the recon-
structed bankers get together, and the manager
of the bank which has intimated that it can’t
meet its engagements, takes the chair and they
pass a resolution regretting the absence of the
manager whose bank is paying ss. in the £, and
then the managers of the banks which were
lately saved by a State guarantee all agree that
State interference in banking is a thing too
awful to be considered for a moment in any
well-regulated community.
• • • •
The history of State interference in Aus-
tralian banking business of late years can be
put in a few words. It includes the propping
up of the Queensland National Bank some years
ago by a loan of sundry millions of public
money ; the rescue of the Bank of New Zealand
from utter destruction by the State assuming
the management and putting in 2| millions to
fill the gap which private management had left;
the rescue of its offshoot, the B.N.Z. Estates
Co., by State cash and State control; and the res-
cue of the Colonial Bank of Maoriland, which is
now confessed to have been drifting into a lament-
able position till the community took it over
also, and enabled it to cast its burden on the
Lord —the only Lord that the private banker
knows in times of adversity, i.e. , the State. It
was the State guarantee which came in to stop
the ’93 run on the Sydney Savings Bank. In
the same year the State indirectly, by means of
the Dibbs Government’s Bank-Notes Act, guar-
anteed the City Bank of Sydney, Bank of New
South Wales, and several others, and whether
the assistance was essential or not, it was un-
doubtedly of great value. The State also in-
formally guarantees the Bank of Adelaide;
every other S.A. bank has gone to wreck under
private management, and when the storm of ’93
raged around the one survivor, it was intimated
that the Government would come to its rescue,
if necessary. It proved not at all necessary,
but the informal guarantee still exists, and pos-
sibly it w'as only the fact that it was ready
which kept it from being wanted. And now
the disreputable, insolvent Q.N. Bank follows
the general example, and falls on the capacious
bosom of the State—as usual.
* * * *
The Bulletin has long expressed the opinion
that the Q.N. Bank was in a bad way, and it
proves, on State examination, to be in even a
worse way than was suspected. The paid-up
capital (,£899,552) is all gone ; so is the alleged
profit and loss balance (£46,995), and the other
alleged reserves—in fact, the whole of the
shareholders’ money, amounting on paper to
£1,182,613. In addition £1,252,810 of the
depositors’ money has vanished, so the shortage
is £2,435,423, and the balance-sheet, which
showed a constant surplus of assets over
liabilities, is to that extent a lie and a delusion.
That is to say, it would be a lie and a delusion
if it had stated that the assets were real and
tangible, but as it merely alleged that the paid-
up capital and the deposits were so much,
which they were, and the advances were so
much, which they were, and didn’t even profess
to assert that the advances were good, or that
the capital wasn’t lost, or that the deposits
were intact, it was, perhaps, about as truthful
as could be expected. Also, the auditors per-
sistently stated that the balance-sheet was cor-
rect “ according to the books,” which seems to
have been quite true. It was just as correct as
the books and no more so.
★ * IP *
Then the report goes on to intimate that even
this awful shortage isn’t the whole of the
trouble by a long way. Many dead accounts
on which no interest is recoverable, and where
the principal is consequently a matter to be
viewed with serious suspicion, have been taken
at their full book value. The assets have been
estimated, not at their present worth, but at
what they may fetch if the bank keeps alive
and they are judiciously realised when
better times come, supposing they do come.
Bank premises, too, are taken at original
cost. It is explained that the alleged profits
shown since the ’93 reconstruction were
largely bogus, made by reckoning in interest
which couldn’t be collected on accounts that
were more or less defunct. The dividends paid
since ’93 were not earned, and whether those
previously paid were all earned or not the com-
mittee declines to say. The provision made for
losses has apparently been inadequate from the
beginning or nearly so, and the Q.N.B. seems
to have been either a wilful fraud or a
sanguine delusion nearly all its life. So far as
can be gathered by reading between the lines
of the report, the institution, if put into sudden
and compulsory liquidation, would stand on
much the same level as the Mercantile, Federal
or City of Melbourne did under the same cir-
cumstances. Nothing is said about the persons
responsible for this ghastly condition of things,
but presumably it was all done by manager
Drury, the Man that’s Dead. The case of the
lamented Colin Longmuir has already shown
the inestimable advantage of having a dead
manager on the premises.
* * * *
All the Queensland National Bank asks under
these painful circumstances is that the old
management which issued the lying balance-
sheets, and lost the money, and paid the divi-
dends which weren’t earned out of the profits
which were bogus, should go on much as usual;
that the shareholders who have no money left
in the concern should still own it; that divi-
dends should still be paid—presumably out of
the deficiency ; and that the old reconstructed
deposits apparently the whole £7,000,000
or more—should be fixed permanently, and
never fall due for repayment at all unless
in case of liquidation, and that the State
should guarantee the payment of principal
and interest for ever and ever, and get
nothing in return. Even the most aggressive
of Eat-Man Governments couldn’t face these
monstrous proposals. But the Government is
prepared to go a long way. It guarantees the
new depositors—about £BOO,OOO worth—for 12
months, so as to prevent a run and give the in-
stitution time to turn round, if such a dry-
rotted concern can turn round without falling


into dust. It proposes that the shareholders
should simply be invited to give up their inter-
est —which, as they have no interest left, is a
small thing to ask —and that, in return for this,
they should be forgiven the £360,000 of unpaid
capital which ought to go towards making good
the shortage. Then the private reconswindled
depositors are to take over the institution and
become the shareholders, and it seems to be
understood that the State is to help them
through by waiting further for its money
and reducing interest to 2\ per cent. As the
Government balance, or at least £2,000,000 of
it, represents money borrowed at about 4 per
cent, to keep the Q.N.B. on its legs, this conces-
sion would mean a State subsidy of over £30,000
a year. Therefore, the State is to lose £30,000
a year, and the shareholders lose £899,000, and
the depositors lose about £1,250,000, and the
loss is beautifully distributed all round.
* * * *
The moral of the Q.N. Bank disaster is the
same old moral. Its leading features are that
balance-sheets are largely waste-paper; that
auditors’ certificates are of no value whatever,
because the certificate tells the same old story
whether things are good or bad ; that private
audits are a mockery ; and that directors and
managers tell the whole exact truth when it
suits them. The Q.N., like every other bank
which has gone wrong, showed a prosperous
and duly-audited balance-sheet right up to the
day when its awful deficiency came to light;
therefore, tlie bank shareholder who relies on
the big, prosperous report of any large concern
with a million paid-up capital and a million
reserves, and a 10 or 15 per cent, dividend and
a liberal balance to carry forward, would do
well to remember that almost every bank shows
things like these till the bottom falls out, and
that his concern, too, may have been insolvent for
ten years past for all the evidence to the con-
trary. And until there is a regular system of
State audit these things will continue to be so.
The Q.N.B.’s request for a State report on its
condition was a tacit acknowledgment that
private audits and balance-sheets are such
well-known lies that they are wholly useless in
an emergency. Further, it was a tacit acknow-
ledgment that State audit is a lot more reliable
than the private article. The banker’s precept
in good times and his practice in bad ones are
woefully dissimilar.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Artificial Boom.
If the thing can be managed it is evident that
Sydney is going to have a giddy, garish boom
about the year 1900. The Reid Government
has two great schemes on hand : the building
of a new Parliament House, which will pro-
bably cost half-a-million or thereabouts, and
the holding of a big Exhibition, which will
most likely swallow another £250,000 or more
of legal cornstalk tender. The Parliament
House, if it comes off, is to be built as usual
with borrowed money, and another slice of
Sydney’s great park is to be used up for the pur-
pose. Considering that one large piece has
already gone for Government House accommo-
dation, and another bit is to be used for a new
railway-station, this third great bite will leave
comparatively little of the once magnificent
property intact. The building of the new
Parliament House and the Exhibition will pro-
vide a lot of temporary work for the Sydney
unemployed, and make things humforatime, and
enable the Government to point with pride to the
activity which comes of a Free-raid regime.
The Exhibition will bring crowds of people to
Sydney and boom city property for the time
being, and increase the business of city shop-
keepers and hotel-keepers, and help to still
further congest population into the bloated
metropolis. And the country districts will
duly pay their half of the bill. The Parliament
House will yield no revenue and be merely a
dead and unsaleable asset all its davs, and
if Sydney doesn’t happen to become the
Federal capital, and the structure doesn’t
come in handy as the seat of the Common-
wealth Government, there will be nothing to
put in it save a species of glorified municipal
council. Most of the country population will
never even see the glittering white elephant,
and will feel no better if they do see it; never-
theless, their share of the bill for interest will
come in regularly for ever and ever. As for the
Exhibition, it is supposed to be paid for out of
revenue, by instalments spread over a series of
years, and the selector in the arid wastes out
back, and all the rest of the struggling, hungry,
joyless bush population, will be levied upon for
their half of that bill also. How the revenue
will stand the charge, seeing that it is already
far below expenditure and keeps declining, and
no provision is suggested for meeting the extra
outlay, doesn’t appear. If the revenue can’t
stand it, it will presumably come out of loan
moneys in the end, as usual. No special reason is
assigned for the present expenditure of about
£750,000 on useless gorgeousness in Sydney. The
alleged policy of the Reid Government is to
“ settle the people on the land,” and to
“ develope the interior,” and to “ open up the
country,” and to “ relieve the congestion of the
city,” and to promote decentralisation, and to
square the finances by strict economy, and
other equally beautiful facts and circumstances;
and the proposal to spend £750,000 or more on
needless pomp in the capital squares very badly
indeed with these ambitions. New South
Wales is miserably poor just now ; it
plain bread and meat, and decent roads, and
irrigation, and more railways, and more indus-
tries, and a better local market for its produce ;
and to give it two hunks of gorgeous architec-
ture and a razzle-dazzle in the city as an equiva-
lent for these urgent needs is mere mockery.
N.S.W. Judicial Relationships.
The gods must laugh a lot at N.S. W. Supreme
Court judicial relationships and their results.
In three jurisdictions—Equity, Divorce, and
Bankruptcy—the judge is practically judge and
jury, and it becomes of the greatest moment to
clients to gain his goodwill. Judge Owen sits
in Equity ; Judge Manning in Bankruptcy ;
und until lately Judge Windeyer sat in
Divorce. Before Judge Owen practises Mr.
Langer Owen, his son. Mr. Langer Owen
was admitted to the N.S.W. bar in ’89 —seven
years ago—as a junior barrister, and he has
acquired a bigger equity practice than any other
counsel admitted for the last 20 years. Before
J udge Manning practises Mr. R. K. Manning,
his nephew. Mr. R. K. Manning was ad-
mitted to the N.S.W. bar in ’B9—seven years
ago—as a junior barrister, and he has acquired
a bigger bankruptcy practice than any other
counsel save one. Before Judge Windeyer
used to practise Mr. Ralston, his son-in-law,
and Mr. Windeyer, his son. Mr. Ral-
ston was admitted to the N.S.W. bar in ’B4,
and at the late lamented judicial demise
of his father-in-law he had acquired the biggest
practice in divorce. Mr. Windeyer, admitted
in ’94—two years ago—had acquired the next
biggest practice. Since Judge Simpson took
divorce there has been, to put it mildly, no
noticeable increase in the divorce-briefs of either
Mr. Ralston or Mr. Windeyer. Judge
Simpson has a son who is available to practise
before him ; but it is understood that he ob-
jects to it, and the son doesn’t practise. Chief
Justice Darley’s son doesn’t practise before
him, for, though Judge’s associate for some
time, he never passed—perhaps he never
tried to pass—his legal exams. Now, no-
body, of course, says or suggests or believes
that there was ever the slightest suspicion of
improper influence as between these fathers and
sons ; but, all the same, clients and solicitors
will persist in thinking that there is some
influence in relationship that “blood is
thicker than water ” —and the matter is not
only a disgust to the briefless and unrelated
Bar, but something very like a scandal
on Justice. Anyway, it is now said that
Chief - Justice Barley, who is out of
the running, has formulated such a definite
objection to the habit that Judge Owen may
possibly be drafted to Common Law jurisdic-
tion ; Judge Manning to take his place in
Equity, Mr. A. H. Simpson to be elevated to
Bankruptcy. Anything will be an improve-
ment on the present state of things ; but, since
the Bar is 'either too much frightened or too
much hobbled to put its foot down in English
Bar fashion, that short and sensible prohibitory
Act of Parliament is badly wanted as a final
settlement.
Melbourne Post-Office Scandals.
A general belief that no punishment worth
mentioning can happen to any influential Mel-
bourne misdemeanant is confirmed in the result
of a Vic. Public Service Board enquiry re the
illicit sale of obsolete stamps by G.P.O. officials.
The enquiry disclosed pettifogging jobbery and
corruption, and serious betrayal of trust among
certain post-officers who drew substantial sala-
ries, not so much for the work they performed
as on account of the trust placed in them. They
were paid to be honest per regulations, and
they broke their contract with the department.
Being required not to sell stamps that had gone
out of public issue, they did sell such stamps
for the advantage of particular dealers, and in
one instance, at least, the delinquent sold them
for his own profit likewise. It was a dirty
business, as far as the evidence threw light upon
it. Probably a more searching and less sympa-
thetic enquiry might have shown it dirtier.
The Public Service Board passed gentle sen-
tences on these gentlemen, convicted of betray-
their trust under circumstances which gave
them no excuse for not going straight. The
highest paid officer of the crowd received
“actual punishment” to the extent of a for-
feiture of about 12 per cent, on his salary for
one year, whilst the chief distributor of stamps,
who was proved to have made money out of his
delinquencies, got off with a fine (payable in
what is practically easy instalments) equivalent
to .£3Bs—his salary being reduced from £485 to
£375 for a period which will entail that amount
of loss. A subordinate officer, who acted in
conjunction with him as a stamp-trader, was
fined £l6, whilst another and almost innocent
subordinate officer was handicapped—as horse-
reporters would say—at two pounds less. The
inevitable snort of public contempt followed
this judgment by the Public Service Board,
after which Premier Turner was requested to
make a few' remarks on the subject in the
House. Premier Turner’s opinion of P.S.B.
justice was expressed indirectly. He didn’t
say that a fine of £9O for the chief offender was
a scandalously cheap get-off for the gentleman,
but he said that the delinquent was no longer
fit to preside over the stamp department, or to
take the position of Deputy P.M.G., which
would have been given to him shortly but
for the recent revelations. As for the most
severely-fined offender, said the Premier, he
was properly shifted to another billet inasmuch
as he wasn’t fit to distribute stamps, and
his accomplice couldn’t be allowed to re-
main in the Income-tax office, “ where secrets
are supposed to be kept,” and would have to
serve the State in some other Government de-
partment. These remarks amounted, of course,
to a suggestion that the Public Service Board is
unfit to adjudicate in important trials of public
servants, therefore the Premier’s confession
that the P.S.B. is a despotic power, whose
sentences the Government is powerless to
amend, was just what The Bulletin has
learned to expect. Every insult heaped upon
common justice in Smellbourne seems to be the
outcome of an unfortunate mistake by which
something that ought to happen (and which
would surely happen elsewhere) is debarred
from coming to pass. Right, in Smellbourne,
is always regretting its inability to rectify
Wrong. Acts of Parliament invariably fail in
the one most important particular. There is
ever a serious flaw in the regulations, a fly in
the legal amber, or something or other that
prevents influential offenders from being satis-
factorily dealt with. And the uninfluential
persons, who don’t offend, are beginning to
want fair play.
A Denominational Champion.
H. H. Champion has broken out in a new place
with a wail for denominational education :
Every serious person knows perfectly well that the
existing State system of secular education is an expensive
failure. Its cost is enormous, and yet plenty of teachers
are bullied as well as underpaid.’ No one claims for it
that it tends to turn out good citizens, and all the evi-
dence goes to show that the effect of the lack of any kind
of religious or moral instruction for the young is disas-
trous to the best interests of the community One
obvious way out of the difficulty is to have an inspection
of religious schools by a State officer, and to allow a grant
of so much per head per annum for each child who
should pass the examinations satisfactorily.
This is most fatuous. No evidence is adduced
to prove that religion or morals can be inocu-
lated by any education system, nor that any
dogmatic system of “ religion ” or “ morals ” is
desirable. Champion’s scheme is still more
weird and weak, because it presumably is in
favor of giving the capitation grant to any reli-
gion whatever, no matter what its system of
morals might be. For instance, Kinsman, the
much-marrying man, is the head of a church, and
would want State aid right away. Then there is
another Melb. parson who has got in much
trouble for greeting the (young) sisters with a
holy kiss, and for inculcating sound religion by
holding the blushing neophytes on his knee.
He is quite willing to start schools for girls
(and boys ?) under 16—and to help to cut out
the State grant. Then the parsons of Orange
lodges would want State aid for their schools,
and a proper hatred for the Pope would thus be
taught out of the taxes. Presently the Mor-
mons of Salt Lake, and the Thugs of India, and
several quaint religionists from mid-Africa,
would come along to bless the name of H. H.
Champion. The idea that religious schools turn
out good citizens is very trustful. A few years
ago some Hawthorn (Vic.) boys were arrested
for a sexual offence. Some sectarian journals
immediately came out with homilies about the
godless system of Vic. education. But the
evidence showed that the boys were all scholars
at one religious school, and were not State
school boys at all. An instance like this shows
the folly of hasty conclusions. N. S. W. is not
“ godless ” in its education system ; it gives
facilities to clergymen to give religious educa-
tion to those children whose parents desire it.
YetN.S.W. crime statistics do not show any
advantage over Vic.—rather the other way, in
fact. But Champion admits that the “ Bible
in State schools ” does not meet his idea. Pre-
sumably, therefore, he wants to abolish the
State schools in favor of the church schools.
How can he make it compulsory for any child
to attend a church school? Vic. found it
necessary to make education compulsory, and
the necessary corollary was to make it free and
secular. Is Champion showing signs of com-
plete surrender to the W.C.T.U. ?
The N.S.W. Gag Department.
Some six weeks ago, in a highly injudicious
moment, and after having for a long time per-
sistently blocked applicants, New South Wales
Department of Alleged Justice promised to
produce the papers in the now notorious case of
Thomas Suffield, undergoing sentence of 10
years’ penal servitude for an assault said to have
been committed on a Lambton servant-girl in
1890. Apparently the Dept, regretted its pro-
mise as soon as made, and for five weeks nothing
more was heard about the matter, though, as
the papers were all together, and no manipula-
tion was required, there was no visible reason
why they should not have been produced on
the following day. If the Dept., however, hoped
that the matter would be forgotten, it was dis-
appointed, and the other day some of the papers,
with various important ones carefully omitted,
were actually laid on the Leg. Assembly
table. Judge Windeyer’s. report on the
case was not presented; nor was the review
of the case which Mr. Walter Edmunds,
counsel for Suffield, wrote at the request
of Justice-Minister O’Connor ; nor a striking
memorandum by Suffield himself (though
Minister Gould’s reply to this memorandum
was carefully included !); nor four important
affidavits, disclosing much new evidence in
favor of Suffield, alleged to have been buried
in the recesses of the Crown Law Office. With
these trifling exceptions, the papers seem fairly
complete. It is usual, with papers of strong
public interest or importance, to print copies
which are held for sale at the Government
Printing Office, and are supplied to members
of Parliament and the press. JVo copies of
the. papers in the Suffield case were
printed ; so the publicity given to the matter
by the injudicious action of the Department of
Alleged Justice will be the smallest possible.
As The Bulletin has already written, when
there is hint of injustice done to any man by
Botany Bay officialdom, all the forces of official-
dom league themselves together to keep the
man in his pigeonhole, the case from the light
of day. The Suffield case, it is considered,
has been definitely shelved for the just-ended
session of Parliament, and by next session
people will have forgotten all about it. Will
they !
New South Wales Government carried motions
in favor of referring to the Public Works Com-
mittee the building of Parliament Houses and
an Exhibition by 50 to 32 and 43 to 18 votes
respectively, and on each occasion members of
the P. W. C. voted with the Government. As
this committee is paid by sittings these votes of
its members can hardly be looked upon as wholly
disinterested. The jobs will be long, the emolu-
ments liberal. In a close division party enthu-
siasm might justify such votes, but, where the
numbers areas given, people are driven to think
that the predominant sentiment of the voters
was greed, and that next and very close to it
was a cynical disregard of the public opinion
they claim to express. The voters were within
their Parliamentary rights. But see the evil
of giving exceptional rights to members !
Tory opposition to the Saturday half-holiday
clause in the Vic. Factories and Shops Bill is
based upon a statement that compulsory closing
will mean ruin to small shop-keepers, wherefore
one councillor after another protests against the
awful injustice of permitting the question to be
decided by a “ bare majority ” on a trades vote.
They suggest, these benevolent old gentlemen,
that the wishes of the bare minority are in dan-
ger of being over-ruled by a combination of
bloated capitalists, who favor the clause afore-
said from selfish business motives. Why should
hundreds of small suburban shop-keepers be
sacrificed to the interests of large firms, asks
Wrixon the Righteous, and Wallace the
gold-bug. Only one councillor had the iutelli-
gence, or the inclination, to remember that
shop-keepers, as a rule, are small, not large. A
bare majority in support of the Saturday holi-
day will mean either that 45 per cent., say, of
these struggling individuals are voting for their
own ruin, or else that the Council “ gag ” is as
hollow as the heads and hearts of its utterers.
It is tolerably certain that the two-thirds
majority demanded by the Council (after 13
members stood out for a three-fourths majority)
will rush headlong to its alleged ruin.
In the interests of Australian humor, it is to
be deplored that the Sargood statue scheme
was not allowed to progress as far, say, as the
getting out of designs. The spectacle on
Princes Bridge of a bronze equestrian figure of
the glorified sock-seller waving his drawn
sword while his warhorse (not warehouse this
time) pranced gaily towards the cathedral
would be a sight to strike fear into the heart of
every foreign invader. Any party which erects
a statue to one of its leaders while he still lives
hasn’t sense enough to come in out of the rain.
Ten years ago it would have needed little effort
to arouse the rabid teetotallers of Victoria to
hoist an effigy of Jimmy Mirams in Collins-
street: and about that time, too, the Orange-
men of Sydney could easily have been persuaded
to perpetuate Francis Abigail in stone or
brass. Per fervid hero-worshippers should take
to heart the rule of the Roman Catholic Church,
by which canonisation cannot take place until
the projected saint has been dead two cen-
turies. By that time his little eccentricities
are forgotten, and the authenticity of any
documents in the case is almost impossible to
prove.
Under-Secretary J. G. Anderson, of Bris-
bane, who periodically whips the Education
Department up there for accidental errors in
literary English, blazons the phrase, “ an edu-
cated proletariat,” in his own calm and well-
considered official report on the state of
N.S.W. Primary and Superior Schools. “Pro-
letariat ” is not and never can or should be an
intelligible English word. “Proletariat!”
wrote a great scholar and philanthropist
on one occasion. “ I distrust the English-
man who applies the term to any sec-
tion of his countrymen. He has picked up
the word without knowing its meaning,
and uses it simply because it is long and
sounds learned.’ Sydney Domain spouters
run the word into every other sentence, and,
with infantile ingenuousness, employ it as a
suitable description of themselves and their
families. Had Mr. J. G. Anderson dragged into
his report “Heredity” and “Environment,”
the influence of the Domain upon him would
stand self-confessed.
Maoriland Tory papers remark, with painful
unanimity, that the Parliament just deceased
is the very worst the province ever had. Look-
ing back on the past 15 or 20 years, the present
writer can’t remember any Parliament in any
Australian province which wasn’t the very
worst Parliament on record, so the above state-
ment was only to be expected. Also he doesn’t
expect that there ever will be a Parliament
which isn’t the worst which Australia has seen
up to date. Likewise there isn’t a live states-
man who can compare with the statesmen who
are dead, and there isn’t likely to be. Yet,
despite the familiar wail of the local pessimist,
The Bulletin reckons that Kingston, Turner
and Seddon can compare without any dis-
advantage to the old politicians that Australia
weeps about so much now that they are dead,
and who were no great shakes when they were
alive ; and that Parliaments to-day are about as
good as they were 50 years ago ; and that the
giants of bygone times were about the usual
size. Looking back into the haze of our cheap
antiquity, it doesn’t seem as if these alleged
giants ever did anything so very enormous after
all. [P.S. —Since the above was written N.S.W.
Parliament has closed up, and the local Star
remarks that it is admittedly the very worst
Parliament that the province ever had. Like*
wise the Argus says something of the same
sort in Melbourne. The very worst Parlia-
ment and the oldest inhabitant and the largest
snake are a trinity, and the great man who is
dead is their near relation.]
The idea of a Government elected by Parlia-
ment is distinctly gaining ground in Maoriland.
The success of the Seddon Ministry is to a
great extent the cause. With the ordinary
kind of Parliament, where the ordinary
Parkes and the commonplace Robertson, or
the average Duncan Gillies and the plain,
dull Jimmy Munro, scramble for office, the
party system is not so utterly bad as it might
appear. The only way in which Parliament
can exercise any real influence over the Ministry
is by throwing it out, but Parkes and Robert-
son and Gillies and Munro were all so much
alike that the Legislature could swap Premiers
once a month and not notice the difference.
Therefore, if the sole means of keeping Gillies
or Munro in order was to throw him out he
could be thrown out without anybody being
noticeably the worse. A system where the
only real influence Parliament can exercise over
the Government is by casting it downstairs is
stupid enough, but if the Government is so
ordinary that half-a-dozen equally good ones
can be found at any time, and so commonplace
That no one misses it when it is gone
then the obvious policy is to cast it
down the stair and say no more about it.
Maoriland, however, is in a different situation,
the Seddon Cabinet is growing somewhat,
bounceable and autocratic as the result of much
success. The country can’t afford to throw it
out when the dreadful Russell clan is the
alternative. Therefore, the Legislature has
less control over Richard of Kumara than over
any previous Premier, and the need for an
arrangement which will enable it to have some
influence over Seddon grows more and more
obvious. The only influence provided under
the present system is to warn Seddon that, if
he grows overbearing, Russell will be put in
his place. But Russell is such a ghastly im-
possibility that the warning is obviously of no
account. As long as Russell is the alternative
there is hardly any bad or corrupt or stupid
thing that the present Cabinet couldn’t do if it
wanted to.
