*’ IJOU . “ A Mother of Three.”
“Francillon,” staged by the Potter-
Bellew co. at Sydney Lyceum last Wednesday,
went straight to the great soul of the audience
as a wicked French play of the most soulful
description. Most other French plays reach this
country in a severely Bowdlerised condition, but
1 rancillon ” arrived practically naked and un-
ashamed, with. beautiful embroidery on its fig-leaf,
said fig-leaf being cut bias and having Valenciennes
round it. The audience was mostly girls, but
as “Francillon” is perfectly new to Australia
they came not knowing the rich and artistic
wickedness of it; if they had known they would
have brought other girls along with them. It was
wntten by the most morally tired man in France
-Dumas the younger; and it does all possible
credit to that brilliant personage. There is a high
social atmosphere about it; the cast includes a
count, a countess, a marquis, and a “ Baroness
Smith ” —presumably the wife of that Sir Smith
who constitutes the Frenchman’s main idea of the
British aristocracy. The story is built on the fact
that the count, who is newly-married to a beauti-
ful wife, goes out almost every night to riot along
with a loose personage whose hair is reputed to be
15yds. long; and comes home with the milk in the
small hours of a.m., with vine-leaves in his hair,
and wine-stains and old cigar-ash on his con-
science, and whoops openly about his sin. Then
one morning a mountain falls upon him, for his
pretty wife straggles in looking blase and worn,
and states that she went out last night also, and
went on exactly the same whoop, and dined and
drank and sinned the same old sin with a strange
man whose name she doesn’t know. She simply
saw him leaning up against a wall and asked him
to come along in a cab and sin—and, not being
Joseph of Canaan, he came. The lady tells this
story to her husband and his two male friends,
and her female friend, Baroness Smith, and they
send out for their relatives to come round at once
and hear further particulars.
* • * *
The lady also wants to know, reasonably
enough, why she shouldn’t sin as well as her hus-
band, and that staggered and infuriated trans-
gressor can only gasp, while his head swells up and
smokes with rage. Nevertheless it proves, after
the lady has boasted eloquently of her sin through
two acts, that she never sinned at all; she is really
a pure and noble woman with a magnificent gift of
lying, and a great capacity for detail. This story
is garnished with much gorgeous furniture, and
there are rich tapestry-hangings all round it, and,
after its own clever, gilded, weary, leprous fashion,
it points a gamey moral and teaches a lesson with
last week’s ash-barrel in it. The same lesson, or
one very like it, may be reached by keeping a cow-
liver too long on hand in the summer, 01 by not mak-
ing quite sure that the prawns are fresh ; and the
moral is mainly to the effect that the dog shouldn’t
be allowed to sleep under the bed into the hot
weather—especially if he is a large, muddy dog.
There are very few dull lines in “ Francillon ; ” it
is bright, clever, and well-constructed; but it
belongs to the yellow decadent school of art, and
there is a prematurely-old, affluent weariness about
it, and when it is over the auditor feels as if he
would like to wash the heroine to make her fresh
again, if that were possible. All the same, it is
the cleverest and best problem play up till now.
* * » *
Kyrle Bellew puts some of his very best
Work into this gaudy shriek of art. He holds the
audience even when he has the stage to himself
and keeps a dead silence; he expresses a
whole series of conflicting plans, ideas and
emotions by dumb show and facial expres-
sion ; and everything that can be done for the
particularly mean hero he does. Cyril Keightley,
as his factotum and fellow-sinner, also reaches his
high water-mark \ his Henri de Symieux is a very
fine performance; and though he isn’t at high
water-mark all the time he is mostly there. The
Countess of Mrs. Potter is a wildly erratic person,
with much of the paint dabbed about her eyes as
usual. She is strikingly good in some places, and
is j ust going to be good in many others when she
flies off the handle, and falls over her elocution,
or commences to whirl herself recklessly in the
Parisian atmosphere. The impression is that
most of the time she is on the verge of something
which she never really is, but whether she would
be any better if she were that thing, whatever it
may be, doesn’t appear. Cathcart, C. M. Berke-
ley, Miss Linda Raymond, and Mrs. Walter Hill
are the other successes of the piece, and Miss
Pollie Emery, in a sentimental character, is about
its most conspicuous failure. Miss Emery is a
born comedienne, and the sentimental girl in the
simple frock doesn’t suit her. “The Ironmaster”
is to follow on Saturday.
** * *
“ Face to Face,” which has taken the place
of the snake drama at Her Majesty’s, started to
very large business ; the late lamented reptile has
been a splendid advt., and if manager Cosgrove
only keeps up the gaudy horror prosperity seems
assured. Unfortunately, the horror already shows
signs of falling off. Beyond the shooting of a
toothless old gentleman in Act 1., and the suicide
of a depraved policeman later on, there is no body
in the present production, barring one which is
mentioned as having taken place some years pre-
viously. The scene is laid partly in Paris and
partly in some other city—apparently London—
and the falling of an avalanche in this latter place
seems unconvincing, somehow. Other things are
unconvincing also. Wilfred Shine, when he is
flying 1 from justice, comes back three times to the
scene of the crime, and stands still, saying in
agonised tones that he must fly, yet he doesn t fly
worth twopence. Also, when he stands off the whole
French police-force with a chair the police don’t
even strike an attitude ; they merely loom up in a
rigid perpendicular and dream. A decrepit English
showman who is taking a caravan round France
and who assaults and dreadfully mauls, on pat-
riotic principles, every Frenchman he meets, and
knocks over large quantities of gendarmes at
Sl ghf> grows distinctly wearisome ; when he
appears at one wing and six French policemen
march in at the other, it presently becomes an
understood thing that he will knock them all down
with one hand and make them humbly apologise
for being alive. And in the last act, when
Shine throws off his disguise and proceeds to
spank the wicked policeman in the presence
of his six or eight large, brutal accomplices,
not one of them even attempts a rescue. They
simply stand up in a row and see their boss being
spanked, and show themselves the most inefficient
crowd of ruffians on record. The fact that the
policeman, after he committed the murder, at
once writes and signs a full account of it—presum-
ably for reference in case he should forget the
details—and spends the rest of his life in a fruit-
less struggle to recover the document, and when it
is lost at once proceeds to write out another, is a
mere casual detail: being a policeman he
would naturally do things like that. “Face
to Face ” is a trifle more irrelevant than
the average, which is saying a great deal;
and the way the incriminating document is
left lying around loose is simply awful to witness.
John Forde, Tom Cosgrove, and Misses Lita,
Mowbray, and Fanny Wiseman are a good deal
better than some of the other people, and an un-
accountable rag infant which is finally dropped
somewhere and forgotten, when it really can’t
keep on pretending to be an infant any longer,
deserves honorable mention. It is the only mem-
ber of the cast who recognises the hopeless nature
of the position and takes the honorable course of
throwing up the contract and dissolving into its
original elements.
** * *
At time of writing (Tuesday, 17th Nov.)
there were generally understood to be ructions at
Her Majesty’s. At all events, one advt. an-
nounces that Williamson and Musgrove open the
big theatre on Saturday (21st) with a brief season
of the “ Trip to Chinatown,” which was such a
specially glorious boom at the Lyceum some time
ago. Another advt. says that William Cosgrove
regrets he can only play the present horror for
ten nights more, and that “ A Mother’s Sin ” (by
arrangement with Billolloway) will follow. As
these two events can’t both happen at once, there
seems reason to anticipate trouble. Rumor has it
that the new management’s representative called
round on Monday to start painting the “ Chinatown”
scenery, and that the old managementdispersed him
severely and told him to get his hair cut. After
that there are said to have been breezy altercations
on the front step and cries of “ Rats ” from the
crowd. The Bulletin has the above on the most
unreliable authority. It, therefore, seems at time
of writing a matter of some uncertainty which
show will appear on Saturday. Her Majesty’s is
a hard theatre to get anybody out of when he
doesn’t propose to go. A former management
hove quite a succession of lawyers and writs down
the steps with great success, and the mark of one
lawyer’s head is still visible where he bumped on
the stairway.
#* * *
Bland Holt has left Sydney Royal, after a
six months’season which can’t have been especially
profitable, for this time Bland has “ blewed ” his
good money on paint and scenery and horse even
more recklessly than usual—and B. Holt, even in
his ordinary parsimonious condition, piles up
scenery and glitter and expensive horse with
alarming disregard of consequence. The Bulletin
also casts the unobtrusive half-brick of personal
friendship after that able manager and reckless
cavalier, C. Simonsen, whom it regards as a
brother, and doesn’t charge him anything for the
loan of the relationship.
** * *
George Rignold takes possession of Sydney
Royal on the 28th with good old “ Henry V ” for
his opening piece. The Royal was the scene of
Rignold s first Australian appearance some 20
years ago, and his return should be an epoch in
local dramatic history—if Sydney is taking any
epoch just now.
** * *
In the matter of Jimmy MacMahon’s Cine-
matographe, public opinion seems to have finally
decided that the skirt-dancer fills the biggest void
under the aggregate waistcoat of the community.
Ihe street m Baris, where everything goes back-
wards, seems to come in a good second. This is
largely owing to the thrillsomeness of the cab,
which is always on the verge of bursting out of the
picture and rushing the audience, though the
bikist who dodges endways across the picture also
exmtes a lot of admiration. Several people have
already had bad falls through trying to bike just
like him.
** * *
The variety co. which blossoms perennially
at Sydney Tivoli, blooms in the spring with all its
wonted refulgence. A special line in the bill an-
nounces that Miss Alice Leamar has returned to
business after a severe illness, and is signalising
her restoration to health by singing “Tooral-lad-
de every night. The Vaidis sisters revolve on
the trapeze as madly as ever, and other things are
much as usual.
** * *
The last night of “Henry V.” at Melb.
Royal was patronised in a well-meant but painful
manner by the local Shakespeare- Society. A
group of elderly gents, wearing unheroic dress
suits and double eye-glasses, seized the oppor-
tunity of King Rignold’s entry into London to
present that mediaeval conqueror with an address
per Dr. Neikl, who, for his part, took the occasion
to make a speech. The speech and the address
would have been appropriate things to happen in
front of the curtain, but, as offerings to King R.
whilst he sat upholding the dignity of his position
upon a white horse, they were out of place. More-
over, the presentation committed another offence
against Art by inducing the heroic equestrian to
return thanks. This disillusion was hard to bear.
The monarch concluded a few remarks by inviting
his faithful barons, and the populace in general,
to give three cheers for “ Divine William,” after
which a 15th century varlet beat the air with a hat
of the period, and demanded a like number of
cheers for George Rignold. Happily the curtain
fell ere they had time to start “ God Save the
Queen. ”
» • * *
Henry Y. scored a point against the Mel-
bourne dailies by quoting a fair sample of the
style of notice served out to him : “ Mr. Rignold’s
performance is so well known that we will not say
anything more about it.” Such a notice does an
injustice to a big show revived after an interval of
12 years. In the case of “Henry V.,” for in-
stance, the performance couldn’t be well-known
to any Melbournians too young at the time
of the previous production to appreciate its
strength. The silliest thing in journalism is
a callow “ critic ” talking flippantly to his
youthful contemporaries about a poetic drama
which became stale to all of them in their
knickerbocker days. Injustice to the show-man
lies in the fact that young fellows are easily
persuaded of their old acquaintance with some-
thing they have never seen, but merely heard
their fathers speak of.
* * • *
The bill for this final week of the present
season at the Royal amounts to managerial revenge
upon degraded popular taste. The respectable
Bard has given place to “Work and Wages,” a
loose-jointed horror in five impossible acts and an
explosion. A cadaverous capitalist figures as chief
offender in “ Work and Wages.” He’s a bad man
and he has a bad time until he drops dead from
heart disease across the spikes of a felon’s dock.
Ihe humiliation of the trial, in which the Judge’s
summing-up lasts twenty seconds, and the jury
don’t retire to consider their verdict, and no form
of oath is administered to the witnesses, proves
too much for the prisoner, who has nothing
left to live for, anyway. He murdered one wife ;
another wife declines to live with him, because he
committed bigamy ; an angry parent is always
shooting at him with intent; his iron-foundry has
passed into the hands of an ex-foreman, who
divides 30 per cent, of the profits among the em-
ployes ; and there are further reasons why the
wretched man should welcome death. “Work
and Wages ” will give a good deal of pleasure to
ij c , unsympathetic majority ” that despises poor
old Shakespeare. It abounds in simple references
to the labor question, and may be taken seriously
or otherwise. Plimmer, H. W. Diver, Sweeney
Frank Harcourt, Prank Hawthorne, and Miss
Hughes deserve an approving sigh; also, a few
people unmentioned are quite capable of doing
better next time.

Critic A. B. Walkley, in the Speaker, on the “ Duchess
of Coolgardie,” at Drury Lane “It shows that if you
are a comic Irishman (with song) you are sure to discover
nuggets of the very largest size and richest quality, and
that as soon as you have pegged out your claim any num-
ber of gentlemen will immediately come forward to buy
it for £20,000, which sum they carry handy in their
pocket-books. What is more, you will be able to detect
the wicked persons in a trice ; local custom compels them
to wear an eyeglass and talk French to simple miners who
haven’t even learned English. ... A young actress, from
Australia, I believe, Miss Hilda Spong, makes a good im-
pression as the heroine, serving out her beer to the rude
miners of Coolgardie with the native dignity of a lady
among the rabble rout of Comus.”
Australia can echo this wail from the Athkn'.kum :—“ A
curious and unsatisfactory sign of the times is the dearth
of young actresses capable of playing serious characters.
In this respect the state of the stage seems worse than it
has been during recent years. Such a state of affairs is
naturally the despair of managers. Promise has once or
twice been revealed, but has been unfulfilled. Rarely has
there been a time when the opportunities for success in
an overcrowded profession have been more brilliant than
now they are. The occasion does not, however, produce
the women.”
Two adjoining shows have come down to employing
ban-ackers on Saturday nights. The rivals stand at the
doors, and in real Richardson style yell the virtues of
their especial mummers into the ears of passers-by.
Douglas Powell, who, after his concert tour, visited the
Westralian fields, left Ivalgoorlie suddenly owing to a wire
notifying that he had been engaged for the part of Lucifer
in “The Golden Legend.” He hurried to catch the Orient
boat at Albany. Powell is somewhat of an amateur miner,
and his patient researches on the goldfields were con-
strued by the miners, who had heard of him as a vocalist,
as the fad of an eccentric Englishman. By the way,
Madame Bessie Cox (Mrs. Douglas Powell) is one of the
principal teachers of music in England.
Victorians will be pleased at the cable re Bertha Rossow’s
triumph on the English concert-platlorm. The late
Madame Patey, when here, was enraptured with the ex-
quisite quality of the young soprano’s voice.
“Billy’ Walshe, the well-known Australian tenor,
seems to have made a great success as Manrico, in
‘Trovatore” at the Comedy theatre, Manchester. He
was called before the curtain several times, and had to
respond to an undeniable encore fob his principal solos.
The melodramas Dampier has been producing at the
Melb. Aleck during the recent ill-fated season, avers a
Bulletin correspondent, are really first-class things in
their line, being streets ahead of many of Bland Holt’s
successes, as plays. With a little editing, good staging - ,
fair cast, and any other theatre but the Aleck, the days of
“ Thou shalt not Steal ” and “ To the West,” would have
been long in the land.
Miss May Glendinning, who was well-known and a general
favorite in Sydney musical circles a few years ago, has
returned from the unmusical North, and proposes to make
her voice—one with much melody in it so far as local recol-
lections go—again heard on the concert platform. Her
business address is at Nicholson’s music-warehouse.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The Bank of Australasia’s report shows a half-
year's profit of £40,568, and after paying away £40,000 in
a 5 per dividend there remains £568 to carry for-
ward. The profit is a considerable falling-off compared
with the four previous half-yearly returns of £41,158,
£40,854, £45,735, and £48,571. The Australasia, however,
has a magnificent cash reserve of over £3,100,000, and
cash and liquid assets together make nearly £6,000,000, so
its position looks absolutely impregnable.
* * * *
The Queensland National Bank, which now
owns up to being an utterly insolvent concern with a
shortage of 2J millions, even on the most favorable esti-
mate, and which wants the Government to see it through,
is just 24 years old. Its original promoter was a banker
from some rather indefinite “ foreign parts,” whose origin
seems to have been uncertain. It was managed almost
all its life by Drury, C.M.G., whose previous record was
that of a very small and unsuccessful branch manager
for an Anglo-Australian institution. It is commonly
reported that the very first entry made in its books on the
first day of its existence was the granting of an overdraft
to a director, but this may have been mere calumny.
The Q.N.8., according to the present appearance of
things, ceased to be altogether sound many years ago.
In fact, it is doubtful if it ever was sound. It seems to
have had a habit of taking a sanguine view of its bad
debts, and regarding its losses as assets long after their
real condition ought to have been apparent. It was run
for years by politicians with big overdrafts, and propped
up with Government money, and, despite the gorgeous 15
per cent, dividends of its good times it would appear to
have been more or less of a lie and a clumsy
“ fake ” all its days. It was known up North in the
old gold-boom era as a most reckless granter of
overdrafts on weak security, and a most desperate
supporter of sad little boom townships which lived
on their alleged “ magnificent future.” The Queens-
land coast is dotted with comatose, sweltering, fly-infested
townships which have always been just going to rise to
immediate greatness, where the inhabitants lived by
selling allotments to each other at exorbitant prices, and
drinking together, and discounting each other’s bills, and
the Q.N.B. used to find the money for nearly everything.
The report that announces the practical insolvency of the
woefully mismanaged institution doesn’t mention that
anybody is to blame for the collapse, or that anybody is
responsible for the fact that the concern is about £2,500,000
worse off than its balance-sheet showed, and there is no
suggestion about anyone being prosecuted. If there
was a prosecution and the whole history was told
from the beginning, it ought to be a most
astonishing and smellful yarn. A bank which is
directed by two ex-Premiers (one of them also an ex-
Acting-Governor, K.C.M.G., and President of the Leg.
Council), one M.L.C. and an influential “Esq.,” and
which was general-managed by the local Commander of
the Forces, and which used to give overdrafts of
£lOO,OOO or so to big politicians, can’t be ripped up
without breaking society into small pieces.
* * * *
The report of the Temperance and General Life
Assurance Society (ordinary department) for the year
ended 30th September is a good deal worse than usual.
Renewal premiums are £27,463, compared with £27,681
and £28,037 for the two previous years, so that the new
business for these two years has all been swallowed up by
claims and surrenders. The new premiums, however
(t'5259), are £l6OO better than in ’95, which is a solid im-
provement—about the only one to be recorded. Though
the income is nearly £2OOO larger this year than last, and
the claims are £l9OO smaller, the amount saved and added
to the funds is only £15,473, whereas last year it was over
£17,000. Working expenses are £10,885, against £8133
last year ; that is to say, the Temperance and General
seems to have collected £l6OO more premiums and spent
over £2OOO more to do it. Expenses this year are fully
33 per cent, of premiums, which is an awful jump
in the wrong direction from the 25 per cent, of last year ;
25 per cent, was decidedly too high, and the present 33
per cent, is unspeakable—or thereabouts. Some of the
items are perplexing ; commission on renewal premiums
goes up £3B, though the amount of renewal premiums is
less; and agency expenses rise at one fell swoop from
£337 to £ll9l.
Interest on investments (.£6783) is less than 4
per cent, on the funds actually invested, which is a very
small percentage indeed. Surrenders, which are always
a depressing feature in insurance business, have increased
greatly. Altogether, there is absolutely nothing of a
cheerful nature in the report, so far as The Bulletin can
see, save the increase in the new business, and that
increase seems to have cost a great deal more than it is
worth. There are too many life assurance societies in
Australasia for the business to be done, and the fierce
competition forces up expenses. The expense rate of the
Temperance and General suggests strongly that it is one
of the superfluous institutions.
The Temperance and General also runs an in-
dustrial department (the threepence and sixpence a week
business) and the necessity of saying something severe
about that department sticks out conspicuously. Pre-
miums (£8242) are £lO2 better than last year, interest
(£1258) is £lB7 worse. Expenses (£4489) are more than
half the premiums; in other words, for every shilling
paid into the society the customer gets less than 6d. worth
of assurance and more than 6d. worth of office expenses.
In ’95 the expenses, for practically the same amount of
business, were only £3301, so in this department also
things have gone the wrong way with great suddenness
and energy. The total funds have declined, as the result
of 12 months’ work, from £35,000 to less than £33,000, so
the industrial business, with a total income of £9500,
didn’t even contrive to hold its own. Altogether, there
is a serious lack of anything cheerful in either department
of the T. and G. Society’s business, and especially there is
a great want of any sufficient results to set against the
alarming increase in expenditure. This remark applies
to the industrial branch even more than the other ; apart
from the charges of the quinquennial investigation it
seems to have bought £lOO more premiums with £9OO
more expenses.
* » * «
The Colonial Mutual Fire Insurance Co. reports
a, remarkably good year. Net premium income (£66,400)
is £5OOO better than last year and £9OOO better than the
one before. The profits are £8789, and previous balance
makes the amount available £13,904. A 5 per cent, divi-
dend absorbs £1875, £lO,OOO goes to start a reserve-fund,
and £2029 is carried forward. The only depressing feature
in the balance-sheet is the return from investments ; the
co. has £37,000 in “ fixed deposits, Government deben-
tures, and bank balances,” and £10,200 in “ loans on
mortgage and other investments” ; total, over £47,000.
Against this there is an income from “ interest, fees, &c.,”
all lumped together, of £1337 7s. Bd. If interest yields
£1330, and only the £7 7s. Bd. is left for fees and &c., even
this only means a return of £2 17s. per cent., which is a
very small yield indeed. Perhaps, if the Colonial would
separate the interest from the fees and the &c. and show
the investments apart from the current account balance,
things might look a good deal better. The Colonial
Mutual Fire Co. is now in its eighteenth year, and is
getting on a solid basis after a stormy career which once
threatened to wipe it out. Four years ago it had no re-
serve and was nearly £20,000 to the bad—the natural
result of insuring people with eyes too close together and
a hooked nose in between —but since then it has pulled
up wonderfully.
* * * *
Melbourne Argus rises to point out that the
Turner Government’s State Bank will burst up Victoria
for good : —“More vicious and unsound proposals could
scarcely be imagined. It is actually proposed to seduce
current account customers from the banks, and to use the
money obtained from them, notin the promotion of trade,
industry, and settlement, as banking money ought to be
used, but partly for the construction of public works, and
partly in the purchase of Government securities. In
other words, if Mr. Turner’s State Bank can wrest
£2,000,000 of current account money from the ordinary
banks, it is to hand nearly all the funds so obtained over
to the Government for employment. Never has the world
before been entertained with such an insane proposal as
that a State Bank shall absorb the floating capital of the
country, withdraw it from profitable enterprise, and give
it to a Government to be spent on Government objects. .
. . Now, let us explain why we call the clauses (particu-
larly clause 83) relating to the ‘ current accounts divi-
sion ’ of the proposed State Bank ‘ wrecking clauses.’
Mr. Turner thinks that the State Bank would attract
£2,000,000 from the other banks. But what batiks?
Hardly the three large banks which successfully weathered
the storm of 1893. . . The three banks in question might
indeed lose a little, but Mr. Turner’s £2,000,000 would be
mostly drawn away from the rest of the banks, whose
current accounts present a total of £4,850,000. These
banks could not, however, lose two-fifths of their current
account balances without having to call in advances to a
corresponding amount. The loss of so much cash would,
moreover, excite great alarm, and further heavy with-
drawals would be made b\' persons who, losing faith in
both the banks and the Government, would hoard. The
inevitable result would be that some of the banks, includ-
ing Victorian banks, would have once more to cfose their
doors, never probably to open them again, and the colony
would be plunged into much greater trouble than it was
by the crisis of 1893. The credit of the colony would also
be destroyed for a considerable time.”
The Argus evidently thinks that cash in the
hands of the State, “to be used for Government objects,”
is a great deal less safe than cash in the hands of private
banks to be used in the private object of giving over-
drafts to people who pay sd. in the £, or to Longmuir, or
Munro and Co., or the Davies land companies. But the
wildest joke of all is the picture of the Vic. reconstructed
banks smashing up generally because a possible £2,000,000
is withdrawn from them and put into the State Bank.
The National Bank alone could spare half the amount out
of its liquid assets, and, as the withdrawal will be very
gradual, at the worst it is hardly likely to be felt. More-
over, the State Bank, in case of a crisis, is pledged to
prop up the private institutions with legal-tender paper
on very easy terms, so if the private banks did lose
£2,000,000 in gold they could get, if necessary, a much
larger amount in a currency which would serve their local
purposes equally well. The State Bank is much more
likely to save the local institutions from going bung than
to wreck them ; and, considering the provisions in
Turner’s scheme for assisting these depressed establish-
ments, the Aegus’s outbreak is a beastly piece of in-
gratitude.
• * * *
Shiels, M.L.A., objects to the State undertaking
the responsibilities of banking on the ground that “ bank-
ing demanded a special aptitude and training, special ex-
perience, and a firm and resolute attitude of resistance to
the influences which would be brought to bear against the
Govt.” Sublime—considering that scarce]}' a week had
passed since a board of directors had escaped criminal
punishment mainly on the ground of their want of know-
ledge of the concerns of their bank, of their extreme
pliability in the hands of a (fortunately defunct) manager,
and of their incapacity to resist his artful pressure.
* * * *
The Vic. Government has decided that it can’t
crowd through its entire State Bank scheme this session,
and it proposes merely to pass—if the Leg. Council per-
mits—the clauses providing for the establishment of a
State Land Bank, and postpone the rest till next session.
Therefore the ensanguined riot about the awfulness of a
State paper currency takes a well-earned rest. This
State paper question has three aspects: (1) The banks
allege that they don’t make any profit out of their note-
issue ; (2) They demonstrate by great masses of figures
that the State must make a loss out of it ; (3) They hang
on like grim death to the allegedly unprofitable privilege,
and, despite their undisguised hatred of the State Bank,
they fight like tigers to prevent it making the loss afore-
said. In fact, so deep and vast is their self-abnegation,
they would rather have the loss themselves.
* * * «
At Dec., 1895, the buildings, goods, and chattels
in the Sydney metropolitan area were fire-insured for
£59,720,282. Of this amount, which represents an enor-
mous premium income, £25,234,000 is in the hands of
English and other foreign companies, and £31,000,000 is in
the hands of companies belonging to places outside Aus-
tralia and Tasmania. The fire-insurance business repre-
sents the curse of foreign capital in. its worst form. The
big foreign co. only needs to bring into the country enough
money to furnish an office, and hang out its honored
name on a shingle, and hump away the profits for ever
afterwards. It gives no security and renders no service
that the local co. couldn’t do equally well, and the local
co. spends its profits in the country and they go into cir-
culation again, and help to keep up the national pros-
perity, whereas those of the English institution disappear
over the horizon and come back no more. It would be
interesting to know how many millions of good Australian
money has gone in this one leakage since the business
began. What fatted M.L.O. is game to start, in his own
interest as a shareholder, an agitation for a heavy tax on
the foreign fire insurance co. ? For this time only The
Bulletin will go with the fat M.L.C.
* * *
Looking back now on the events of ’93 Victoria
would give about £1,000,000 in hard cash to have had
Dibbs for Premier then instead of the ghastly Patterson.
Dibbs’s bank-note legislation and issue of State paper
saved two Sydney banks from going ker-wallop and let
the smash down easily; Patterson lost his head and
allowed things to go. If the Dibbs legislation had been
applied in Melbourne the National, Victoria and Ballarat
banks would never have suspended, and the difference
that would have made would have been tremendous.
N.S.W. is grateful to Dibbs —so grateful that it promptly
threw him out of Parliament. But he is to manage the
amalgamated Savings Banks at £2OOO a year.
* * * *
Just now Sydney is full of people who have
come from the West by cheap excursion trains. Few' are
in a hurry to return to the heat and flies out-back (w'rites
a Bulletin correspondent); consequently many return-
tickets to Bourke, Cobar, and other places, are, despite
law-an’-order, available at cheap rates. Registry-office
keepers buy many of these tickets—often as low as five
shillings. The ordinary fare to Bourke is £2 10s. 2d., and
registry-offices are almost daily despatching servants to
the West. They provide the tickets and deduct the full
price from the Westward-bound governess’s or slavey’s
magnificent salary of 10s. a w'eek or thereabout.
For concentrated gall, take the average mining-
expert. Two of that ilk, who have been examining S.A.
mineral country, lately blasphemed Premier Kings' on and
co. in the ever-available columns of the Tory press because
S.A. Govt, declined to allow mining drummers to travel
“ on the never.”
* * * «
The ticks, droughts, and other plagues are
playing sad havoc with northern station properties. The
other day was sold Glenvale station, area, 640 sq. miles ;
rent, £3OO per annum (paid in advance); 10,000 cattle and
140 horses, with numerous improvements, for £l5OO (3s. a
head). Situated about 140 miles from the Towers—an
excellent fattening run. The mortgagee lost a cool
£lO,OOO over the sale.
* * * *
The S.A. Govt, intends to organise a comprehen-
sive scheme for advancing seed wheat through district
councils to the farmers who have been ruined by
, drought. The figures of the present season read thus :
Sown for wheat, 1,500,000 acres; cut for hay, 500,000
acres, estimated yield of wheat from the remainder
2,000,000 bushels ; needed for food, 1,970,000 bushels;
wanted for seed, 1,500,000 bushels; shortage, 1,470,000
bushels. Ordinarily, about 3,000,000 bushels are exported.
* * * *
“Sentinel,” from Gundagai, N.S.W. : “The
mines here having been persistently boomed, the inevit-
able result has followed. Poor men, with wives and large
families, heard of the new Eldorado, hastened hither, and
are now huddled on the river flats, in many cases under
calico not sufficient to cover a large wheelbarrow. Day
after day they look for food by the wayside. Another
rush is now taking place on account of the new bridge,
just started, at which there is little chance for unskilled
labor. But there is no law to punish the boomster who
encourages this state of things.”
* * * *
The magnificent report on the Girilambone
copper-mine, N.S. W., has been followed by intimations
that the shares are selling at 10s. each, and are recom-
mended at that price. It is also declared that before
many months the mine will be paying regular divs.
Capital £50,000, which is nominal, as the shares have been
issued as paid up to 17s. 6d. That does not furnish much
money to work a copper-mine. “ A sample of surface ore,
broken off by Mr. Bennett in my presence, gave 23 per
cent, copper by assay.” Nor is that the way to sample a
copper-mine.
* * * •
Sofala, N.S.W.: “ The monthly escort left this
morning with 3600 z. of gold.” About 12oz. a day—not
enough to keep Sofala in drink !
* * * «
Great news at last from Mt. Gahan mine, Pam-
bula : “ The Co. has received a special medal and certifi-
cate from the World’s Fair Exhibition at Chicago.” Why,
in the face of this, are shares being forfeited for a 3d. call?
“ Damn Chicago ! ”
* * * *
Barraba, N.S.W. ; “The famous Dodger mine,
Crow Mountain, has crushed 23 tons of stone yielding a
total of 210 oz.” But how many crushings have they had
this year ? If the mine is famous it is simply infamous
not to keep on crushing.
Overflow mine, N.S.W. : “Struck rich oxide of
copper bottom of main shaft about 18in. wide.” It would
need to be fairly rich. Muudy, a well-known copper-
man,. is examining the property, and John Howell
follows. It is time something definite was known about
this show. Some heavy talk has been done about it,
chiefly by interested squatters, and the average squatter
knows about as much of mining in any form as the
average miner knows of sheep.
* * * *
What is the celebrated Woodstock mine doing ?
What has become of the Demondrille ? These mines were
going to do very big things.
* * * *
Cabled that the Perseverance G.M. Co., to work
a mine at Stuart Town, N.S.W., has proceeded to allot-
ment. Capital, £112,500. It will want all the virtue of
its name to pay on such a ponderous capital. The mine
may have a future ; its past has not been up to much.
* * * *
The Howell’s Consolidated G.M. Co., announced
in last-week cables, is to take over mines known as the
Dust Hole and Just-in-Time, at Junee, N.S.W., and the
Prince of Wales, near Gundagai, on the recommendation
of John Howell. As nothing has come from Howell about the
two-foot vein of “almost solid silver” alleged by the papers
to have been hit in the White Rock mine, the news is a
little too soon, and must be put down to wind or whisky.
* * * *
The Empress of Coolgardie mine has struck
something at last : “ A big deposit of cobalt and nickel
ores. There are some thousands of tons of ore in sight.”
Not a soul will believe it, so, if true, every chance will be
given for those in the know to buy scrip.
* * ** *
Quite a booiny report wired across by the chair-
man of the General Gordon mine, W.A. : “ Samples taken
promiscuously from drive averaged over soz. to ton.
Prospector’s shaft : Samples from drive gave over 21oz. to
ton.” Crushing-expectations are now centred on next
month, and everything about the battery is going on all
right under the engineership of Allison Smith, formerly
locomotive-engineer of Vic.” The market did not respond
to this report. The public, whatever the G.G. mine may
turn out to be, have very little faith in the crowd running
it.

Great Boulder—7lB tons, 21580 z. Kalgoorlie

* * * *
From Menzies: “Things are very dull here.
The announcement that 80 miners were wanted to open
up a number of reefs at the 4-Mile was the signal for a big
rush. I had a stroll over the ground, and saw nothing to
justify expenditure. One of the biggest things here in
the way of spending money is the Menzies Group. A big
collapse here for a number of mines as soon as the bat-
teries get to work.—B.S.”
* * * *
A deluge at last. Kalgoorlie: “The Brown
Hill Extended has struck a supply of water estimated at
85 gallons daily.” Not a word about “the pumps are
working well,” so as to assure people who have relatives
there that all danger of a flood has been provided against.
Gov. Gerard Smith, of Westralia, talking at
Melb. Mayoral banquet last week, advised Easterners not
to be led into believing that all the mines of his province
were “ what are vulgarly termed 1 white cats.’ ” People
present wanted to laugh.
* * * *
A recent Westralian law decision makes legal
managers of mines responsible for workmen’s wages.
There is talk of appealing against the decision, but just
think what a fine old time an invisible company and an
irresponsible manager might have if the law were not as
laid down !
* * * *
“ Kalgoorlie,” says a Hannan’s correspondent to
The Bulletin, “ has gone to the dogs. The Stock Ex-
change and commercial business are dead, and unem-
ployed fill the streets. Wages are down in every business,
trade and profession. Miners are getting now only £3
per week in some places—without water. The old cry of
‘ Wait till the railway comes’ being ‘ hoff,’ the boomsters
are now shrieking ‘Wait till the general exemption is
over.’”
* * * *
The Westralian Govt, may, to all appearance,
be compelled, ere long, to provide relief works for the
unemployed in Wild-Cat Land Yet laborers for Westralia
—in batches of 100 at a time—are being advertised for in
Melb. papers ! Is this merely a paradox —or something
worse ?
* * * *
After some good crushings and-one or two divs.,
the Premier mine at the 25-Mile, Coolgardie, is to be re-
constructed, to provide £lO,OOO working capital. Some
40 miners were recently put off. Outlook blue.
• * * *
Maritana, Kalgoorlie, not yet sold and tha
chances very remote. Two extensions of time granted,
and a third now wanted.
* * * *
W.A. Govt, has decided on the usual exemp-
tions—six weeks for all the fields save those in the Far
North, which are allowed eight weeks. This will give
breathing time to hundreds of worthless working mines,
throw thousands of men out of work, will not be availed
of by the gold-producing mines, and will in a number of
ways prove the great richness of the mines that can’t
afford to work. The exemptions are a God-send to the
Ministerial and other boomsters with W.A. mines for sale
in London. The man who wants to sell a mine usually
has to work it, and if lie gets six weeks exemption all the
gold is left in the mine for the new company.
* * * *
The much-vaunted Big Blowis,orhas been idle—•
under labor suspension conditions. And what has become
of the Australasian mine, within a couple of miles of Cool-
gardie Post-office, which, according to that awful person
“ Smiler,” had in sight enormous quantities of lOoz. rock ?
Tub Bulletin hopes that no one has walked away with
that mine.
* * * *
Clarke and Co., of Adelaide, have just issued an
exceptionally full number of their periodical Shark List
and Investors’ Guide, also a supplement dealing with
Westralian stocks. The Bulletin doesn’t endorse Clarke’s
theory that the inadequacy of working capital provided
by Australian promoters is “at the very root of the re-
cent market collapse,” but a quantity of solid information
in the circular will he found very useful.
» * * *
Stated in Clarke and Co.’s monthly circular,
which, by the way, is incomparably the best stock-and-
share list in Australia, that very conflicting reports are
flying about regarding the General Gordon mine, W.A.
Interested persons have declared it equal to the Great
Boulder. This may be dismissed as a cram. Others say
good gold went down some distance, and that the
stringers in the formation were very rich, but the mine
has not responded well to further development. The
market has accepted the latter version. Why not get
some well-known and disinterested man to examine and
report? Whatever the parent mine may turn out the
G.G. pups appear to be a most unpromising lot.

From Cooktown : The leader of a New Guinea
prospecting party declares that the published accounts of
gold-returns from there have been exaggerated—the total,
so far, is about 900 oz. He would lie sorry to see a rush by
men badly equipped.
* * * *
The output of silver in Bananaland is also
slightly on the increase. 1895 gave 225,0190 z., yielding
£30,042 as against £22,077 obtained from silver in 1894.
Nearly all obtained from Herberton and Ravenswood.
Tin is in a bad way, 1894 only turning out 2871 tons, worth
£102,277, as against 2114 tons, worth £68,133 in 1885.
Total value of Queensland tin up to date, £4,325,771.
Melb. Herald announces that the directors of a
recently-victimised mining co. will not take criminal pro-
ceedings against the persons alleged to have salted the
mine, owing to legal difficulties and the monetary respon-
sibilities such proceedings would entail. Is it, then, to
be understood that in Melbourne, if any person contem-
plates committing a crime, he has only to make any
attempt to convict him sufficiently expensive to enable
him to set about his crime without any misgivings as to
gaol ?

for South Australia, and his big things, on paper, have

famous Dodger ? Why doesn’t it crush ? To keep a mine

Last week a young Sydney solicitor (newly
fledged) was made a Commissioner for Affidavits
by Chief Justice Darley, said young attorney
being a son of ex-Judge Windeyer. And yet
there are more than 20 solicitors of older standing
than the new appointee down on the list awaiting
appointment as Commissioners.

beaten at a recent Melb. city council election, ex-
pressed surprise at his defeat. Local newspaper
men say that Mr. Anderson might yet be in the
council had he not once remarked, or been
credited with remarking, that “anybody could
square a pressman with a pint of beer. ”

STIMULATING JUSTICE.
THE CLERK : “ Prisoner, you cure charged with
being drunk and disorderly. How do you
plead? ”
PRISONER : "Not Guilty; and I desire to know
whether it is the wish of the Bench that I am
to get a fair trial? ”
BENCH : “ Certainly. You will get British justice
and fair play.”
PRISONER : “Then, as owing to circumstances I
shall have to defend- myself, and my nerves
are too shattered to enable me to do myself
justice, I must respectfully apply for an alco-
holic stimulant before proceeding with the
ease.”
A N.Q. country paper, a few days ago,
stated that its respected townsman, Mr. E. J.
Bottlenose (who was seriously ill), was slightly
better when they went to press, and that he would
most likely pull through provided fermentation
did not set in. Now, most of the residents knew
that Bottlenose lived on it, so, whilst some won-
dered if it meant inflammation, others laid wagers
as to how much over-proof it would be.

A Tasmanian lodge calling for applications
for the lodge doctorship received one tender
actually offering to attend members’ families at
the rate of 2s. 6d. per member per annum.
* * * »
Queensland’s present agony grows out of
whether or not Governoress Lamington should be
addressed in society as “ Her Excellency. ” It is
learnedly pointed out by a churlish faction that
the Constitution Act provides but one representa-
tive of Her Majesty, and that as Lord L. is
one the Governoress would be two. This seems
cogent, but when Jersey was in N.S.W. he in-
variably used “we” as indicating the representa-
tive body at this end in all social affairs.

* * * *
Pastor Fitchett is working up for the Review
of Reviews a history of the Adelaide Hospital dis-
Eute, and he aspires to put both sides fairly. If
e succeeds he will woefully offend the “ striking ”
doctors who, from the first, have been flagrantly
in the wrong.
* * * *
The Hospital trouble has turned Premier
Kingston’s hair grey. It all arose because Miss
Gordon (now Matron of Perth Hospital) was made
superintendent of night nurses over the heads of
some senior charge nurses, the exact advance in
salary being from .£52 to <£7o a year. A big fire
from a little spark.
* * • •
The disaffected doctors, having vainly ex-
hausted every other means of defeating the Govt.,
are now trying to persuade the Adelaide Hospital
nurses to resign. Only gross ingratitude could
permit of such a step, for the Government’s whole
The late ALEXINA MAUDE WILDMANN (“Sappho Smith ” of
“ The Bulletin")
Marion, photo.
®ffht has been on behalf of the nurses, whom the
late Board and the late honorary medical staff
sought to keep in subordinate positions while their
lady friends took the higher and better paid
posts. Strange to say, there are still scores of
applications for every vacancy on the nursing staff.

Napier’s deadliest enemies, when they come across

* • * *
The epidemic of hospital rows produces a
case at Albury in which the medico alleges that
the matron’s buying biscuits without his authority
was the source of offence, while the matron counter-
allegeS that the persecution against her originated
in a refusal by her of two offers of marriage.
The striking Adelaide doctors were very-
anxious to refer the hospital disputes to a commis-
sion presided over by a Judge, but two Judges
declined, and the other, Chief Justice Way, is a
brother of Dr. Way, one of the leading strikers,
and ineligible.
• * * *
The hospital problem intrudes into every-
thing in Adelaide just now. Aid. Puller, who
opposes Tucker in the Mayoral contest, is a mem-
ber of the National Ass, and the opposition to
Tucker is with the object of depriving him of the
hospital-board membership, which is an appanage
of the Mayoralty.
* * * *
Yic. Gov. Brassey, in the rdle of the
Humble Governor:—
My constitutional position is one which is most con-
genial, and demands of me what I am most happy to dis-
charge—the duty of believing, as Ido believe, that the
majority in Victoria know a great deal better what is best
[or their local interests than I can possibly know.
Bike journalism, you notice, is fearfully
and wonderfully aggressive in its advocacy of
biking, whilst every creature who rides a machine
seems to be a rabid missionary trying to ram his
new gospel down the throats of the unconverted.
In point of fact, confirmed bikists are unsober
persons—intoxicated with the exuberance of their
own velocity—and not to be taken seriously. They
protest too much that they are deriving new life
and happiness from the exercise, whereas appear-
ances, at the end of six months or so, suggest that
their original condition of comparative ill-health
and misery suited them better. This is certainly
true of many girl-riders, “improved ” from plump,
kissable charmers into wan and weary ghosts of
the past.
Talking of “ cyclodonahs Melb. Herald
published some highly suggestive letters on this
delicate subject awhile ago. The writers in every
case, being very anxious not to say what they were
actually stating in so many words, reminded one
of the discreet Crackles. They knew enough to
make them blush, but they weren’t agoin’ to tell.

* • • •
Among the influences making for an Exhi-
bition at Sydney in 1899 is a semi-official estimate
that, apart from manual labor, over 1000 billets will
be created in and about the building. As there
will be at least 20 applicants for each billet, all of
whom regard themselves as likely to succeed, an
army of 20,000 Exhibition-claqueurs at once stands
forth. Another influence strongly in action is
that of those who see titles, from C.M.G.’s up, in
the business.
A STEP TOO FAR .
DISBETT ROBINSON (to Barnacle Jones) : “And why did our friend Barnacle Smith leave
the Red Tape office?”
“Ah, well, you see, he used to come in, put his name down, and go away. But he got care-
less, and wouldn't come and sign the look, you know. The other side put him
away, so he resigned with a compensation. Deuced hard on the poor devil.”
Attorney-General Isaacs, scared at the sug-
gestion that he looked with a lenient eye on Jewish
religious and charitable art unions, has been sooling
the police on to illegal gambling in all forms,
The profitable “ race-game,” which has been the
best money-making machine in vogue at carnivals,
bazaars, etc., is rigorously forbidden. One result
has been plaintive appeals from church-bazaar
runners to be allowed to use it, but the invariable
reply is that no exception can be made. Mr.
Isaacs thus gets back on the people who insinuated
that he was partial,
„ 4 *
The bosses of Melbourne Benevolent Asy-
lum seem to be about the only persons down South
who have a sincere objection to cheap labor.
SOME SYDNEY CHARACTER SKETCHES.
Lately it transpired that certain toothless old
gaffers in the institution made a few pence at
mending boots and sharpening razors, and that
some of the ancient crones eked out sundry cop-
pers at stocking-knitting. The proceeds were
spent in gaudy dissipation—principally tobacco
and tea. One member of the board, when he
heard of this, said “it was an appalling state of
affairs,” and his colleagues became practically
speechless with horror. When they partially re-
covered it was decided with emphasis that any he
or she patriarch in the asylum found doing any-
thing in future would be thrown out,’and have his
or her skull indignantly bounced on the pave-
ment. The asylum inmates are in future only to
sit and mumble at each other, with their one
remaining hair hanging down their backs, and all
boot-repairing is suspended under pain of death.
* * * *
Round the stinkful Merri Creek, Melb.,
the gentle Chow works in the gardens on Sundays.
A big policeman does special duty and keeps off
the local youth from molesting the heathen. The
secret is that the boss Johns are up at the church
praying and being slobbered over by the gentle
white maiden.

A Northern (N.S.W.) correspondent to
The Bulletin: —
Apropos your oft-repeated warnings re the danger of
j’-oung girls being allowed by their parents to attend
Chinese mission meetings, already within a radius of four
miles of a Northern town there are three half-bred Mon-
golian infants, the offspring of Sunday-school teachers or
their near relatives. An almond-eyed set of twins has
also caused trouble in the family of a one-time strong
supporter of Chinese missions. One alleged convert,
when asked by a local police-sergeant his opinion of the
mission business—" Parson tarn fool allee time ; hut welly
ni gell allee time, too."

A Southern divorce lawyer lately con-
ducted a suit for a plaintiff wife, got a decree nisi,
and recovered =£llB from funds left in the pro-
vince by the erring husband. He then brought
in his costs against the wife at £ll9, but he had
the decency not to ask for the balance due of £l.
And yet people are crying out for law reform !
* * * *
There was trouble lately between a N.S. W.
shark and his victim re costs. Shark at last
offered to take £5 ss. and added that if the victim
was not satisfied the shark was willing to refer
the amount of his costs to any decent firm of
lawyers in Sydney. The reply:—
Herewith cheque for £5 ss. I note re your charges. My
fortunately limited experience has taught me that the
more decent a firm is the more they charge. For example
they work it out this way
Amount of law supplied £0 10 0
Decency ... 4 15 0
Total £5 5 0
Yours, &c.
* * * »
The other day, at Sydney quarter sessions,
on behalf of an elderly man accused of having
assaulted a young girl, it was pleaded that having
formed the habit of drinking methylated spirit he
was at times unaccountable for his actions. The
N.S.W. Customs returns indicate that methylated
spirit is extensively drunk in Sydney. Some years
ago, when a Minister, B. R. Wise, was seeking
re-election for South Sydney, the duty on methy-
lated spirit was reduced to Is. per gallon, as a sop
to the furniture trade, strong in that quarter.
Result, a great increase in consumption, not
attributable to any cause except its use as a
beverage. It was at one time supposed that spirits
were rendered undrinkable by methylation, but
modern science has got over that little difficulty.

A Humble Governor must be
A rare and precious sight to see !
The meanest of us knows that he
Is fit a Governor to be—
Yet, if a Governor he were,
He’d be as proud as Lucifer.
(How good to think you’re cleverer far
Than common—noble—Governors are!
And that there are in you innate
Abilities supremely great —
Which, if they once could get a show
Would paralyse some folk you know !)
And so, again we say it—we—
A humble Governor must be
A rare and precious sight to see.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Nov. 10, 1896.
My Dear Myee—
Bowral and Moss Vale habitations are filling up
prematurely this season. In seeking mountain
retreats for the summery weather, the Very Nicest
never underrate the importance of having their
fresh milk from cows reared on pastures adjacent
to the Govt. herd. A loyal high-toned public
finds out first in what country district the hens
will cackle over the vice-regal breakfast egg, and
flit in that direction accordingly. Subsequently
the spot is declared to be the only possible summer
resort in the whole continent.
A Vic. mountain locality, which enjoyed a
boom of years because it was patronised by the
Lochs, slumped a little under the Hopetouns, who
preferred the western districts of the province,
but has now recovered its tone, and is declared by
many Sydney folk superior to anything this side,
and they are off to it for the summer. The
attractions of the place consist in driving up and
d >wn a steep hill-track in a turn-out smart enough
to make all the other residents restive, wearing all
your best clothes to neighboring afternoon teas,
and dressing for dinner. The only sport—for
women —is to play the precedence game for months
at a stretch ; that is, strive your very hardest to
get in the clique above you and utterly snub and
flout the ambitious stragglers trying to raise them-
selves to a higher bracket of the social Walhalla.
I lie men find their amusement in staving in town
and only running up to the Mount from Saturday
till Monday. Fish, fruit and vegetables are all
sent up from Melb. at famine rates, but every-
body on the Mount can afford to pay high prices,
and it is a blessed abode of calm, dull, respectable
“ gentility ” where the very cows have a gently
ruminative air of consciousness that the ultimate
end of their existence is whipped cream.
The Brasseys have just given a fashionable
fillip to Bright by rushing rapidly round the place
on bicycles a few weeks ago, and alternating that
with snow-shoeing. But tli ire is too much energy
required by tourists to make Bright a summer
residence. A tour in those parts means stiff
climbing and strong boots and serge, and a total
indifference about the straightnesj of your fringe.
The picture of the fashionable promenade in
Vienna, shown by the new cinematographe, tells
us that we needn’t hesitate about ordering new
and expensive blouses for fear the fashion will
have changed before they are made up. The
blouse and-skirt costume still reigns in that modish
city as it does in outlandish places, where it helps
along civilisation by enabling its wearers to look
smart with little trouble. A correspondent tells
me that in the Wild Far West the women turn
out every day in the main streets of the towns as
cool and composed as they would in G-eorge-street
on a winter’s day. The men, arrayed chiefly in
dust, swear at the heat and mop their faces; the
women trip out of their tents neat and trim in the
universal blouse and skirt. They may faint, but
never perspire.
One of Sydney’s prettiest girls, married some
time ago, when on a trip ’time, to a man with a
historical name, has returned for a short visit to
her native town. Like all young matrons, when
they want to enjoy themselves, she has left hubby
and the baby behind, and, disentangled from her
responsibilities, means to have a good time.
Sydney is becoming the haunt of the newly-
married from Vic. Quite lately, numerous annex-
have been made from among our next-door
neighbors’ sassiety damsels, who prefer to carry
on the matrimonial battle on the shores of the
beautiful harbor. Two bridal couples have arrived
from the South within the week—Mr. and Mrs.
Hinchcliffe, married with much pomp and cere-
mony among the tree-ferns and giant gums of
Lilydale a few weeks ago, who have taken up
their abode at Garden Island ; and Mr. and Mrs.
Phillips, who will settle here. Mrs. P. was Miss
Rosenthal, of Melb.
Sydney’s new Ladies’ Club is just opposite a
matrimonial agency. I merely draw your atten-
tion to this as a detached and lonely fact, for there
is absolutely no inference to be drawn. Marriage
rates in ladies’ clubs are much below par. There
is an abandon-hope-all-ye-who-enter-here sensation
lingering about the portals that never leaves one
for quite a while. The Melb. Austral Salon is a
shocking- example. There, most of the members
on the original roll remain as they were. The
male associates are so in altogether a Parliamen-
tary sense. A Salonite wedding with another
Salonite is as unthinkable as a fourth-dimension.
They haven’t the marrying bias like the Tween-
ways.
A preliminary meeting of Salon members was
held on Friday afternoon in the Club rooms; Mrs..
Burdekin and Mrs. Gadsden, the hostesses, shook
bands with as many early arrivals as they could
reach, and late comers had to be content with the
staircase. Not much scope is given for larger
meetings, when a crowd of 54—the four are men—
fill the rooms to overflowing. An entertainment
of music and recitals went on in the small drawing-
room, and afternoon-tea was administered in the
dining-room.
People are still marvelling at the sale of Dr.
Wadham’s furniture which took place last week,
and at the wonderful value placed by buyers upon
his household gods. The bargain-hunter was quite
out of it on that occasion. Some embroidered
Irish table-linen sheets brought three guineas a
pair. Of course, these fancy sheets are not for
sleeping on, any more than the highly-ornate
towels seen in some richly-gilt abodes are for
wiping damp hands on. They merely elaborately
conceal the working material below. Dr. Wad-
ham’s house had been most sumptuously furnished
as a private hospital. Authentic Persian carpets
of great age—all genuine Oriental floor-cloths
have a past—carved-oak fitments also a century or
two in arrears of present day, sticks and luxurious
hangings, all fetched top prices.
Affected people, I observe, seldom put sugar in
their tea. It is “ high church ”to drink the after-
noon cup unsweetened. Girls who are sweet-
toothed enough to nibble a sugar-lump on the quiet
will make a sacrifice to style and a wry face over
the afternoon ceremonial cup. Why doesn’t some
enquirer unearth the etiquette of tea-drinking ?
Most men pile sugar into the cup until it contains
a kind of syrup. Bushmen seldom take milk even
when they can get it. At a very swagger garden-
party in Sydney lately a distinguished globe-trot-
ting guest, around whom the shivoo had been
principally built up, was offered sugar, and para-
lysed the profferer by replying in icy tones, “No,
thanks, I nevah take sugah two days running.”
“Francillon,” at the Lyceum, is a comedy
setting forth the joy experienced by a “mahrried
mahn ” in Paris, and his wife’s discovery and
plotted revenge for the same. It follows that the
aforesaid wife must be young and lovely—an old
and ugly helpmate is only deserted in a farce —and
must wear captivating clothes. The “ mahrried
mahn ” who enjoys himself on the naughty con-
tinent must always be rich enough to do the thing
in style, dress his wife in seal-skin, employ pow-
dered menials, and call his aged parent “ Markee.”
Mrs. Potter’s Francillon, legally owned by Kyrle
Bellew, whose giddiness causes all the trouble, in
her costumes shows Worth at his worthiest. There
is Paris in every stitch of them.
That blue morning gown worn in the second
act is quite the best thing Mrs. P. has yet done on
the Australian stage ; it is of the faintest-hued
silk draped in a marvellous manner from between
the shoulders with canvas trimmed with inserted
bands of lace. The sleeves are of a brighter blue
with black spots, and a little band of brown fur
round the neck caught with a topaz gives snap to
the whole. Fortunately there is much less reeling
and writhing and fainting in coils in this part
than in others Mrs. B.P. has done, and she can
stand up straight and show her gowns throughout
the piece. She makes a distinct creation of each
separate black gown she wears. She conies a
good way out of her first-act frock and stays
outside it most of the act, but that is a Potter-
ism to which a gasping d.c. has become accus-
tomed, and it is a lovely frock with a front ar-
rangement like a Watteau pleat gone wrong. Her
cloak is very Frenchy, with a tight velvet bodice
underneath the full magpie folds. A figured black
and pink silk with black chiffon fixings reveals a
fetching pink silk and white lace underskirt, and
her skating coat of sealskin with large sable cape
suggests immense wealth. To see Mrs. Potter’s
I rancillon is a liberal education in clothes.
The Lyceum stage-dressing doesn’t reflect itself
in front. Dowdiness reigns around the d.c. most
depressingly, even on Saturday nights, which are
supposed to be sassiety functions. A globe-
trotting lady of title, with adherents all in niourn-
ing garb, filled one of the boxes last Saturday.
On Monday, with a sparse circle, all the boxes
were taken ; in one sat a well-known family group,
casting roses adoringly before the fair Cora.
The Bland Holt farewell on Monday night was
a great affair. “Joe” and Mrs. “Joe’’manage
to get themselves beloved wherever they go, and
they don’t need to pump up enthusiasm at “ Good-
byes.” When they depart a crammed theatre is
sorry, and says so. Parting flower-gifts were on a
quite gigantic scale, and Mrs. Holt had a sort of
informal reception in her dressing-room when the
many friends went round to say “ au revoir.”
I riends of the late Mrs. Carl Fischer want to
raise a memorial fund to her, to be devoted to the
education of her grandson, Carl Andrew. Her
library, including all the collection of musical
works, will be auctioned shortly.
An up-country visitor, strolling past the grand-
stand at Flemington, noticed a very pretty girl,
and, in his artless way, inquired of a couple of
Johnnies standing in front if they knew who that
swell girl was. The Johnnies were struck dumb
at the stranger’s cheek, and froze him with their
stony glare. Presently he learned that his divinity
was the newly-arrived sister of a nobleman, and
that the two Johnnies were A.D.C.’s doing watch-
dog over the Governor’s box.
All ills of mind, body, and estate can be legally
cured by women. She-preachers and she-doctors
abound, and “ proputty,” up till now man’s most
sacred possession, is, in America, litigated over
under feminine supervision. Mrs. Nellie Coke, a
Californian lawyer now visiting Sydney, numbers
among the most successful women practising at
the bar those who go in for civil and probate
business and office practice. Portia before the
Full Court is not such a triumph. Even her
upholder admits that she wants “ a few genera-
tions ” of training. A public patient enough to
be bored and have business obstructed during the
lady barrister’s evolutionary stages must console
itself by reflecting that it is suffering for the good
of posterity.
French is an attractive language to dress up a
par or a shop sign, but it presents many pitfalls.
The ambitious dressmaker, with window lettered
“ Miss Dash, Parissiene couturiere et costumiere, ”
gives herself away as much as the restaurant with
the legend affixed, “ Ici en parU francais ”
where they serve “ table de hote.” Local spelling
in the mother tongue is decidedly shaky too.
Two dentists advertise themselves recklessly as
“Messers” Smith and Jones. Also, a town
tradesman labels his wares as the “chepest,” and
“ monies ’’ are temptingly offered to borrowers on
several signs. After many years, the “Peir”
hotel is being brought up-to-date in its ortho-
graphy. Some of these guileless errors are pho-
netic, and follow the twang of local speech, and
some are reminiscent of “ the ole congvick d’ys,”
as Sydneyites call them.
They were talking nationalities in a drawing-
room recently, and loud above the hum of conver-
sation rang the voice of an intensely superior
sassiety dame, who keeps an 18-year-old daughter
in short kilts to make herself juvenile, “ Oh, deah
no ; Ai’m not a colonial. Ai’m English to the tips
of my fingahs.” The company paused, visibly im-
pressed, when the silence was broken by the quiet
voice of an old lady seated in a far corner : “Yes,
my dear, I can prove that. I remember your poor
father and mother arriving 45 years ago, and you
were a big child then.”
The harmless necessary “ souvenir ” is super-
seding the nearly played-out wedding-present as
a form of blackmail. It is worked thus : Mrs. A.
sends round a circular letter to all her acquaint-
ances, urging them to contribute towards a small
keepsake for “ dear Mrs. 8.,” who is leaving for a
trip ’Ome. To find a sufficiently complacent Mrs.
A., the souvenir-receiver must previously have
shown some practical civility to her social en-
dorser. Intended victims have shown signs of
jibbing at the latest testimonial-raising, and some
have gracefully wriggled out of adding their mite
to the heap. But the recipient knows not of this,
and urges her backers to beg laggard male friends
to contribute, as they are only “ waiting to be
asked.”
A craving for culture has lately been developed
among some Sydney girls. They meet .on Monday
afternoons to drink tea and have facts fired into
them concerning Rossetti, W. Morris, and other
soulful poets, whose poems they recite and read,
after listening to a lecture on the same. The lec-
turess, a grad, of Sydney University, is a daugh-
ter of the late Alex. Britton, and niece of
“Timotheus” Willoughby.
The women suffragists are holding meetings in
suburban town-halls, and are excited and jubilant
about the almost certain success of the “ Great
Principle.” When they get it, as they have already
in S.A. and M.L., it won’t be on account of any
enlightening eloquence on the part of fluent plat-
form women who have devoted their lives to the
“ Cause,” but because it may happen to suit the
political cards of some party wishing to use the
woman’s vote for its own purpose. The tyrant
man gets ahead every time. Moving spirits bring
fervor to bear on the glorious enfranchisement of
the down-trodden sex, and thrill with triumph as
they mop their heated faces after a fervid appeal
to the slave of centuries to arise and demand her
rights. She might keep on demanding if it did
not suit some mere man in the back-ground to
grant it her. There’s nothing wrong with woman
suffrage as a principle, but only listen to the
speeches of the men advocating it to find out how
little they care for that principle ! The cleverest
man can be made a fool of- by the silliest woman,
but it takes merely an ordinary man to twist the
cleverest woman round his finger.
Three songs by Aug. W. Juncker have been
published by Wicken and Co., of London ; two,
“All in All ” and “Love’s Last Appeal,” were
previously published locally. “ Galilee,” the new
one, is a song of the popular sentimentally-
“ sacred ” variety like the little polka that became
the choir-boy, with fluttering angel’s wings and
organ effects in the accompaniment and a sacred
calm pervading the last verse. It may safely be
recommended to the most serious family for Sun-
day afternoons. “ Nazareth ” has much to answer
for. The map of Palestine is searched for geo-
graphical names calculated to give a religious thrill
to song-titles. Composers will get to Kadesh
Barnea and Ramoth Gilead before the demand
gives out.

“ A fashionable Melb. modiste is the first lady
member of the Victorian Racing Club. It was
submitted by her proposer that she was specially
entitled to the privilege, as her skill and talent,
embodied in the taste and elegance of the toilettes
emanating from her firm, and displayed on the
Lawn, had, for years past, materially contributed
to the success of the various race-meetings by ren-
dering them especially attractive to the ladies,
who were, after all, the main support of these
social gatherings. The claim was at once cordially
recognised, and the first lady candidate unani-
mously elected amid great applause.

The Late “Sappho Smith”: A Memory.
The portrait on page 10 of this issue represents
Miss Alexina M. Wildman, known to all Bulle-
tin readers under her pen-name of “Sappho
Smith,” whose untimely death last Sunday morn-
ing will be regretted by everyone who is acquainted
with her writings. Miss Wildman’s whole liter-
ary career was associated with this paper, and her
connection with it covers the best part ot The
Bulletin’s history. She made her first appear-
ance in this office as a bright, audacious school-
girl with original ideas and strong ambitions, and
she was a distinct literary success from
the very beginning of her career. For a
little over ten years Miss Wildman was
a regular member of The Bulletin staff;
and, even after this lengthy period of hard work,
she closed her life at the early age of 28 years.
The letter signed “ Sappho Smith ” was probably
the most widely-read portion of this paper, and it
fully deserved all the popularity it achieved. It
demonstrated so fully the deceased lady’s abilities
that it would be superfluous to say anything
further of her capacity as a journalist. Miss
Wildman’s fatal illness had its first beginnings
early in 1894, but until four months ago she per-
sistently remained at her post, and maintained her
brightness and energy unimpaired. At last she
was induced to take a holiday, and she returned
from a visit to Queensland only to die. Her last
contribution appeared in the issue of Aug. 22. Miss
Wildman used to say that it was her desire to pass
away on a Sunday, and she breathed her last very
peacefully just five minutes after midnight last
Sunday morning. Her remains were laid in
Waverley cemetery close beside the sea, at the
quietest end of that interminable city of the dead.
The Bulletin deeply regrets her as a brilliant
writer, a good comrade, and a lady who was loved
and esteemed by all who knew her well; and is
sure that its readers all the world over will share
in its regrets.
Coincidence : Just died, within a few
weeks of. each other, Faber and Hardtmuth, the
world’s biggest pencil manufacturers.
Time deals gently with the woman who
uses “ Babeskin ” soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. Thos. S. Loney, 208 William-st., Sydney.*
The story goes in Sydney that neither Mr.
Edmund Barton nor Mr. R. E. O’Connor is willing
to accept the vacant Supreme Court Judgeship—
which has been declined by Mr. C. B. Stephen
mainly on the ground that he has a brother on the
Bench. At time of writing, the question who
would succeed Mr. Justice Innes was still a matter
of speculation.

It was characteristic of Verdon that nobody
ever saw him with a walking-stick, and yet eleven
curious ones were auctioned at the sale of his
effects.
The South Australian Parkes, H. T.
Morris, of Anlaby, who has been 60 years in S.A.
and is 81 years old, was wedded 18 months ago,
and—the marriage has not been unfruitful.

The will of Baron Von Mueller is simplicity
itself. The entire estate left by the old man —the
accumulation of a long life-amounts only to £1036,
The Baron devotes the interest on <£loo to deco-
rating his grave with flowers.
Leith Napier, the imported doctor so much
discussed in Adelaide just now, is, like his chief
supporter, Premier Kingston, a big, burly man,
standing over 6ft., and looks capable of settling
the hospital difficulty in one round, were it pos-
sible to settle it by fisticuffs.
The chief constituent-trouble which one
N.S.W. Minister of the Crown at present staggers
under is the importunate solicitation of a wealthy
J.P. supporter who begs the Government to affix
his name to some newly-created township or some
newly-discovered river.
Dr. Thornton, Bishop of Ballarat, has,
according to the London correspondent of the
Argus, quite recovered his health, “and could
return to his diocese should any urgent occasion
arise for his presence.” Calm !
The Bulletin deeply regrets to hear that
the malady by which Mr. J. P. Garvan, of the
Citizens’ Life Assurance Co., formerly Treasurer
of N.S.W., is laid low, has been pronounced to be
malignant disease of the stomach.
Miss Portia Geach, the 16-year-old Prahran
(Vic.) art-student who lately carried off a five-
year scholarship of the British Royal Academy, is
the first Australian to thus succeed. She is a sister
of Edwin Geach, the theatrical manager.

know he was the father of colored children ” !

£. £. Smith advertises the 37th year of his
medical almanac publication, in his 44th year of
practice in Victoria. The corky little man, who
is now within three years of seventy, ascribes his
general juvenility and clear skin to the habit of
taking a small dose of sulphur in water every
morning. Perhaps it is as well, apart from health
considerations, that <£.=£. should be fond of sul-
phur.

Old Baron Von Muel-
ler delighted to relate
how, when director of
Melb. Botanical Gardens,
he entered into conversa-
tion with an utter stranger
who was contemplating
the grounds from an
eminence. The stranger
was voluble and rattled
off botany by the yard,
never pausing long enough
to allow the director to
correct his many errors.
At last he summed up
the scene before him :
“Yes, ’m, ’m yes; very
fair gardens. They’d be
decent gardens if it wasn’t
for that blanky old fool,
Von Mueller.” “ Mine
vriendt,” said the Baron,
more in pity than in anger,
“lam dat blangy old vool.”

Gaoler M‘Kinley, of Wellington (N.S. W.),
who died suddenly the other day, was one of the
three police officers who engaged in a hand-to-
hand encounter with Angel and Thurston, bush-
rangers, at Belinfante Bridge, near Adelong, when
storekeeper Stewart was murdered and the two
outlaws shot down.
Physiology Professor M‘Coy, of Melb. Uni-
versity, has been granted a year's leave on half-
pay, J 2575. Had the University had legal power
to award a pension, the Professor would have re-
tired thereon two years back. The present half-
pay is by a legal fiction a yearly allowance, which
is really a pension save only in name. The work
of the physiology department will be carried on by
a lecturer at <£soo a year, with tacit right to the
reversion of the chair. It is an open secret that
the lectureship will, if he applies, be given to
young Dr. C. J. Martin, of Sydney. Dr. Martin
is a Bachelor of Science and Bachelor of Medicine,
of London University, and a M.R.C.S. For a
considerable time he was demonstrator and lec-
turer on physiology at King’s College, London,
under the celebrated Professor Halliburton (whom
he assisted in his textbook of chemical physiology
and pathology), and he now occupies a similar
post in Sydney University under Prof. Anderson
Stuart. Dr. Martin’s researches on blood coagu-
lation, on the action of black and tiger snake
venom on the blood, and on the poison-glands of
the platypus, have gained him high distinction in
the scientific world.
Barrister Purves, who forgot the Lord’s
Prayer, takes the same gallery as Col. Burnham,
one of the United States successful lawyers, when
things religious come up. “You don’t know what
Christmas celebrates ? ” cried Burnham to a
witness, whose sanity he desired to impeach,
“ The jury can judge what head a man has who
doesn’t know what Christmas celebrates ! ” Then,
turning on the witness again, “ Don’t you know,
sir, that Christmas celebrates the crucifixion of
the Redeemer ? ” But the slip lost Burnham the
case.

last ride behind four black horses. Everybody
knew flus., and all persons of importance—except-

funeral arrangements would be carried out by the
Collins-street coffin-maker. His business during
boom-time became so large and lucrative that a
scheme for converting it into a limited co. was
about to be realised when depression set in, and
the prospectus, if The Bull-tin remembers
rightly, was withdrawn. One way and another
