the bursting of the boom affected Augustus con-
siderably, but he preserved a cheerful countenance
and wore his black doth bell-topper jauntily to
the end. A good-hearted undertaker, was Gus.
Sleight. If his customers proved to be stiff in
both senses of the term, he didn’t press for the
bill, and many a time he must have lent a “fiver ”
to a sick acquaintance previous to burying him
gratis. Not a vain - glorious man, either. The
pomp and vanity of a fashionable interment didn’t
dazzle him, who had stage-managed so many im-
posing processions. Gus. might have buried him-
self in gorgeous style at the smallest possible cost
price, instead of which his funeral was strictly
private “in accordance with the wishes of
deceased.”
Dear Bulletin, —l knew the Edward
Grennan, now of Victoria, who has just (after 42
years !) received a pension for having been one of
the Balaclava six hundred ; and, putting aside the
fact thsft without cast-iron proof the War Office
wouldn't have “ parted,” the Hibernian warrior’s
account of that splendid piece of insanity was eon-
vincingly straightforward and graphic. “ Divil a
thing I remimber about it afther the ‘Charge’
till I finds meself slashin’ away at the Rooshaa
gunners ; an’ how I got back I know no more’n
the dead ! ”—Ex-Vio.
“Wally” Kerr, a 26-year-old
Sydney native, whose defeats at Mel-
bourne on Saturday came as a suf*
prise, is one of the smallest and
most remarkable of Australian cyo-
lists; he stands sft. sin., and, stripped,
weighs Bst. 91b. He is a game and
clever racer, paced or unpaced ; he is a
most consistent performer, and always
does better in the actual race than in
a trial, partly because he has the pluck
and mostly because he is not a cash-
amateur. He recently went for the
paced Aus. records for quarter-mile to
one mile. He flew the quarter through
in 26 4-sseo., as against Englishman
Harris’s 27 3-ssee. and Don Walker’s
27 ljssec.; he scorched the half-mile 08
in 54sec. dead—Jack Parsons’ record;
and he threw the whole mile behind
in lmin. 57sec. ; Parsons’ Australian
best is lmin. 58 3-ssec. Kerr’s best mi-
paced mile against time was 2min.
17sec. ; the difference, 20sec., between
this and the paced mile is the exact
difference which pacing used to
make to Zimmerman. Like most
successful cyclists, Kerr began in an-
other line of athletics ; he rowed con-
stantly from the age of 13 to 18. He
began cycling at 19, and, after seven
weeks’ learning, he rode second-last in
a novice race. Since then he has raced
on all sorts of bikes, on all sorts of roads
and tracks, against all sorts of com-
petitors ; he had but one serious fall;
it broke his nose. He has gained some
160 trophies, including many bracelets ;
and no one girl has more than one of
them. He has toured N.S. W., Vic., M. L.
and Tas. He was the first man to cyole
across IS .S. W. Blue Mountains. He thinks Christ-
church, M.L., with its beautiful roads, a cyclist’s
paradise ; but for a real comprehensive tour Tas.
comes first with its scenery, roads, fruit—and
girls. Kerr goes to England next March to race
against the English amateurs, and he hopes to do
well there, since he has always found a cold climate
suit him better than a hot one ; he has always
raced better in his native winter than in summer.
In England, too, he will have the time and oppor-
tunity to train systematically. Training, so far,
he has believed in one ride daily, one walk, one
quarter hour’s dumb-bell exercise, one short swim,
one sun-bath on a beach ; three square meals.
“ Billy ” Briggs, author of the Age’s and
Sydney D.T.’s straight and clever articles on the
Westralian goldfields, who, as a stenographer, is
unexcelled, has joined Vic. Hansard staff. He
was at one time first violin on Melb. Theatre Royal
orchestra, and in his early years worked as a miner
on Bendigo. Verily a good all-round man.
The resurrection of lunatic Creswell re-
calls the fact that editor Brentnall, of Brisbane
Telegraph, in his old Wesleyan days, married
the Tichborne Claimant, according to that vera-
cious party’s original affidavit, to Mary Ann
Bryant, in Wagga in 1865. The claimant then
called himself Castro, and naively explained that
he turned Wesleyan for the occasion, as he was
thereby enabled to lie about his real name.
Apropos the lamented death in England,
on 2nd Oct. last, of Dr. S. M. Caffyn, whose
graceful sympathetic stories have charmed many
a Bulletin reader, “ G.G.M’C.” sends the fol-
lowing :
IN MEMORLAM,
STEPHEN MANNINGTON CAFFYN,
OBT. 2nd OCT., 1896.
The star in the sea finds a sister,
The swan crowns her mate in the lake,
And the wave where the pale moon has kissed her
Is silver once more for her sake ;
But the willow that waves o’er the water
That mirrors her image in vain
Sighs in accents Despair must have taught her—
Ah 1 never again !
Whom the Gods love, they all die too early,
We miss their glad faces too soon
From our world in its mad hurly-burly,
At morn and at night and at noon.
How we love them (of Life avaricious) !
How we clasp them in death to our hearts !
While the crowd and.canaille meretricious
Probe the wound till it rankles and smarts.
He has passed in the prime of his power,
He has sped in the dawn of his day ;
In the time when the sun-gazing ftow’r
Of his genius tower’d high o’er the clay,
Now silenced his laughter for ever ;
All his sallies and sweetness are past,
His like, it returns to us never—
To think that that smile was the last 1
We loved him and loved altogether,
Physician, Philosopher, Friend,
Through the fairest and stormiest weather
With never a thought of the end.
Yet he dies not: his story survives him ;
He lives in the tales he has told
With a glamor all golden around him ;
He is ours ! and he never grows old.
Yet, waft breezes! o’er sea these bright flowers,
All glad with the gold of our sun,
And lay them ’mid tears from soft showers
On the mound where the grass has begun ;
Lay them there o’er his breast in Old England
For me and for us and for mine,
Where fond children and wife yet lament him
“ Twixt Shadow and Shine.”


"NONE SO BLIND,” ETC.
“Already the great revival of trade which has followed the U.S. Presidential
campaign has been claimed in several places as a proof of how a protectionist victory
affects the country. To a political party which saw in the return of Major M'Kinley a
protectionist triumph it should be easy enough to think thus. The simple truth is that
the immense increase of activity in commercial circles which is now evident has nothing
whatever to do with any fiscal change that may result from the late election.”
—Sydney D.T.

morally certain that it isn’t the Protectionist victory! ”

One of the best known Queensland journalist®,
writes a letter to Warner’s Safe Cure Co., Mel-
bourne.
“Gentlemen: For two years I suffered from
indigestion, and various medicines and doctors
could give me no relief. My appetite fell away,
and I dreaded taking a meal. I lost strength and
energy, passed sleepless nights, and was compelled
to resort to opiates and stimulants in order to
fulfil my daily duties. Life contained no enjoy-
ment for me. Then one day, about a year ago,
Warner’s Safe Cure was recommended by a friend
as a certain cure. I had little hope, so many
medicines having failed, but decided to give it a
trial. After taking one bottle a change in my
health for the better became apparent. I con-
tinued taking this health-giving preparation for
about a month, then the world changed for me.
The sun shone brighter. Sleeplessness vanished,
and life was a torrid zone once more. You have
my permission to make use of this, as I consider
by recommending this medicine that I am confer-
ring a boon, not on its proprietors, but upon suf-
fering humanity.”
WILLIAM FLEMING,
24 CLARENCE STREET, SYDNEY.

N.S.W. Leg. Council, for the first time in
its existence, sat throughout a whole night of last
week to wind up the business of the session.
N.S.W. abolished newspaper postage in
1835 —the year the Imperial authorities re-
linquished their claim on the local land revenues.

Governments exist to execute policies, and Oppo-
sitions exist to execute Governments.” —Melb.
Age.
How unfortunate that some people cannot
believe in any possible dissociation of Dirt and
Democracy ! Over the Temple of Labor they
would fain inscribe the words : —“ Abandon soap
all ye who enter here ! ”
A propertied Upper Chamber amends the
legislation of the elected Lower Chamber. And
we, enraptured at the performance, brag about the
constitution in which the National Belly sits in
judgment on the National Brain.
Required the exact temperature of the hot
words required to make the average legislator
blush. Can it be that certain politicians are
prawns found on the sands of corruption, which
never redden until they’re boiled ?

ment for throwing out the Local Option Bill on

jab it into the Upper House about next May.
A N.S.W. District Court Judge asserts
that Public-schools breed criminals, immorality,
&c. Let Coghlan answer : —Prom ’6l to 95 the
population has more than trebled itself, while the
number of school children has increased 6f, times.
At the same time crime has only increased by one-
fourth, while the rate of committals shows a posi-
tive decrease.

In the General Federal Assembly, fore-
casted by a select committee of N.S.W. Leg.
Council in 1857, N.S. Wales, Victoria, S. Aus-
tralia and Tasmania were alone represented.
Queensland did not then exist, and W.A. was a
Crown colony. Federalists to-day should be
happy to start with the old name-basis.
The beginning of the trouble. Recently
arrived in Aus. waters the Yamashiro Maru, the
first vessel of a powerful Japanese steamship co.,
with a crew of five European officers and 87 Jap.
sailors. The apartments are scrupulously clean
and the cabins commodious, while the vessel
carries 14 guns, and oan, in 20 minutes, be con -
verted into a veritable sea-hornet.
Bananaland has a Public Service Act, de-
signed to eliminate political influence from the
Civil Service. In the new Bill before the Assembly
a clause actually exists allowing the Minister to
over-ride the Public Service Board’s decisions !
Motto for the civil service all over Australia :
“ Beware of using outside influence, unless it be
powerful enough to get what you want.”
M‘Kinley, the U.S. President'
elect is also a Bimetallist, but not of
the Bryan variety. MMvinley believes
in an arrangement whereby all the
civilised nations will make silver and
gold equally legal tender to an un-
limited extent, the fixed relative value
being the same everywhere. The Bryan
party proposes that the U.S. should
make silver a legal tender without
waiting for other nations to move in
the matter, which would mean that the
States would have to buy about all the
silver on earth that was not otherwise
required, and wouldn’t be able to sell
it again. The M‘Kinley idea of Bi-
metallism is feasible enough; if all
nations came to an agreement they
could make anything legal tender. The
Bryan variety looks—in view of the
fact that the U.S. Treasury is already
crammed with silver which won’t cir-
culate —like utter insanity.
Among the several hundred or
thousand or billion candidates who are
now raging in Maoriland there isn’t one,
so far as The Bulletin can find out,
who doesn’t profess to believe in the
anti-borrowing theory. Also, there
doesn’t seem to be one who is prepared
to advocate the only policy that can
prevent borrowing, which is to invent
some new sources of revenue, by taxa-
tion or otherwise, which will yield (say)
.£500,000 a year ;to let the proceeds be
paid direct into a Public Works Fund
so that the Treasurer can’t absorb them
into revenue ; and to give up profess-
ing to depend on the sad, precarious
surplus. The surplus, as a means for
the construction of new railways and
roads and bridges and harbours, isn’t
worth 2jd as a general thing.
Absurdly illogical for Premier Reid to
ukase that “sweater” manufacturers shall not
tender for Government contracts, when his own
Freetrade policy sucks in “ sweater ” goods by the
thousand. Can’t he ukase something against the
importer whose “sweater” imports compel the
manufacturer to “ sweat ” ?

The Yarra-bank orators anticipated the
“Trafalgar” meeting at Melb. Town Hall by
passing a resolution that the Democracy of Vic-
toria, being opposed to all jingoism, “tenders the
right hand of fellowship to the French Consul,
and through him to President Faure, with a mes-
sage of most friendly feeling towards our brethren,
the people of France.” This was proposed by
the irrepressible Fleming, seconded by a parson,
and carried unanimously by about 1000 furious
republicans. It was a grimy hand of fellowship
that they held out to President Faure, and it
hadn’t done much hard work lately, but it was
quite as honest as the average paw that applauded
Brassey and his himperial offspring next night.

early closing. He says that if he—Wallace-had
never been compelled to work more than eight
hours a day he would not now be a member of the
’Ouse. The Bulletin rise? herewith to deplore
the fact that there was no State legislation for
Wallace in his younger days.

A Northern correspondent to The Bul-
letin :
Nelson’s and Tozer’s protest about so tew Chinese com-
ing into Queensland is all rot, as they come in all along
this river (Duinaresq) without let or hindrance, and so
confident are they that there is no objection that they
enquire boldly on the W.S.W. side where to cross over.
They cross openly in daylight at Texas, where there are
police stationed, but the officers evidently have the
official tip to be blind. And why? Well, people get
from H to % of the tobacco crops grown by the Chow.
This year most sold from 6d. to 7d. per lb. A good crop
may go from 1500 to 20001 b. per aore, which means a nice
thing for the lazy whites who employ them. Some people
here who only pay Id. per acre per annum to Govt, for
land, make as much as £lO per acre out of Chows.
One man, who can neither read nor write, made about
£lOOO last season. Advance Australia !
The one genuine brick which the Maoriland
Tory party can throw at the Seddon Government
is that it is a borrowing Minis-
try with an anti-borrowing
policy. There have been
many anti-borrowing Min-
istries inAustralasia, but
they all borrow. The
live things that the
“kawl in ist ” is
solid about is that
this is the richest
country in the world,
that it has more
wealth per head of
population than any
other place on earth,
that its resources are
“ boundless,” that it
can't stand enough
taxation to pay its
own current ex-
penses, and that, if it
doesn’t keep borrow-
ing from poorer coun-
tries abroad, it will
go bung and be para-
lysed, and the Lord
only knows what will
become of it.
It is suggested that
Chief-Justice Griffith’s
visit to New Guinea has
something to do with Q.
colored labor difficulty. Three
or four years ago Griffith made a
similar visit, and discussed with Ad-
ministrator Macgregor the feasibility of
using Papuan instead of Kanaka labor on Q.
plantations. But Mac. objected. This time
Griffith is said to have in view a scheme for the
transfer of the plantations themselves to Papua.
It is an audacious idea, but could only apply to an
extension of the sugar industry, since it would pay
none of the Q. planters at present making a profit to
shift, and Boodlewraith will stick by its bank,
whose mortgagees the planters are. Then what
would be the attitude of white Australia to a
white-and-black New Guinea ?
Apparently the awful revelations by Ander-
son “ agin ” Slattery—-the Dibbs Govt.’s Minister
of Mines —re the drunks and incapables and dis-
orderly ignoramuses said to have been appointed
to civil-service billets, have fallen through. At
least, a select committee, more or less superin-
tended by Slattery’s ex-colleague Lyne, reports, in
effect, that Anderson now says he was “ misunder-
stood,” and that he has nothing against Slattery.
This lame conclusion of the most picturesque story
ever told about the N.S.W. Public .Service is very
unsatisfactory. If Slattery’s appointees were not
as drunk as originally supposed, how drunk were
they, or were they not drunk at all ? And if they
weren’t drunk at all, who was drunk ? Consider-
ing what Anderson said about Slattery, the com-
mittee can’t well whitewash Slattery without say-
ing something very nasty indeed about Anderson.
And, if it doesn’t, people will be apt to think that
it lets Anderson down easily for fear that he should
retaliate by saying something more about Slattery.
Duncan Gillies, it is alleged, will be an-
other candidate for the Yic. Speakership. Dear
old. cordial Duncan, with the shiny countenance,
which always looks as if it had just been washed
with brown soap at the kitchen sink, would
probably make a good enough Speaker in his way.
He never quarrels, but affably throws his own
natural grease on the troubled waters, and no man
can back-down more genially than Duncan.

from the Maoriland

Of the 19 Labor Ms.L.A. in the Q. As-
sembly only one sports a “hard-hitter”; the
others wear the homely soft felt or cabbage-tree,
and by a coincidence the one mentioned has
yearnings for the loaves and fishes to be obtained
on the right of the Speaker.
Premier Turner is reckoned the quickest
and clearest speaker in Vic. Parliament. He is
reputed to have got through his speech on the
State Bank Bill at the rate of 100 words a minute,
yet he was clear and distinct, and never lost h-is
way once among great masses of intricate figures
and details. Deakin is a good second as a quick,
business-like orator. It isn’t necessarily the man
who has a lot to say who occupies the time of Par-
liament, provided he knows his subject and is
dear and rapid ; the man who mumbles slowly
along and says “er ” and “ahem ” may look con-
cise in Hansard, but he fills in a dreadful lot of
time.
A Bulletin correspondent :
Instead of bringing in a Bill to extend time for pay-
ment of Land-tax, why could not Treas. Reid have intro-
duced one enforcing payment of land-tax on amount re-
turned by landholders within 30 days from passing of Act?
The difference between Commissioners’ valuation and
owners’ could then be adjusted satisfactorily and the
balance due to Govt, paid on the court’s decision being
pronounced. The Govt, could hold a prior right to land
for balance of taxation in a similar manner to muni-
cipalities. Should the owner not pay promptly on
court’s decision, the Govt, to summarily sell land for
balance of taxation due. If the land did not bring balance
of taxation at auction it would clearly prove that the
Commissioners and court had much over-valued it.
There would not be one such case in 1000, as the
Govt, could take the right to bid, and thus prevent any
ring among owners being formed. I suppose this would
afford too satisfactory a solution, of the difficulty for
Reid. The time he has allowed for hearing appeals will
not suffice for half of them. —Country Cousin.

A remarkable Refrigerator for keeping
butter, meat, and milk cool during the warm
weather is on sale at Messrs. Brandt Bros.’, 236
Pitt-street, Sydney. It is a metal cylindrical
chamber ; water is placed in the bottom, which
requires replenishing once or twice a week; by
capillary attraction it is drawn up so that the air
can play on it, which makes it self-acting, causing
the water to chill making the interior quite
cold. The hotter the weather the colder it gets,
as the evaporation is greater.*
The Eye-sight, measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Full
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra-
clough. *

In Vic. Leg. Council, last week, J. C.
Campbell gave his little reason for objecting to
early-closing legislation and all stuffanonsense of
that sort. • He told his brother fatmen that when
he was managing a rag-shop for the almighty Sar-
good he used to work from 4 a.m. until midnight,
and it did him no harm. That’s where Campbell
falls into pitiable error. It did him a lot of harm
by helping to make him the sort of man he is to-
day. The down-trodden counter-jumper, as soon
as he “rises to a position,” is the most intolerant
of mortals.
Mr. John Shaw, of Parramatta, to The
Bulle.iv : —“Edward Poynter, new President of
the British Royal Academy, had a. Frenchwoman
for mother. An uncle of his was the sculptor De
Triqueti (one of Louis Philippe’s barons). His
father, Ambrose Poynter, - an architect of note,
who drew exquisitely in water-colors, was a mas-
ter of ‘ perspective,’hence the present Poynter's
force in that line. E.P. was at Lee’s school of
drawing, London, for a short time as a boy (after
leaving Westminster). His father’s sight failed
about ’5B, and the family went to live at Dussel-
dorf, where the future President studied hard.
Paris, however, was where he ‘ graduated ’ as
Master of Art. He and I were chums as boys in
holiday times, which he often spent at my father’s
place in Kent. He was specially smart at fire-
work-making, and together we turned out won-
derful rockets and Roman candles, one of which
exploded in his hands and went near to ruining
his eyesight.”
San Francisco Chronicle says of Maxine
Elliott’s petition for divorce from her husband,
George A. M'Dermott: “ The plaintiff s attorneys
have reason to believe that the suit will not be
contested. Mr. M‘Dermott has found another
who pleases his eye, and the divorce will be mutu-
ally agreeable.” Is the man mad .
Dr. Grace Clara Stone, who headed the
poll against all-comers for the Prahran (Vic.)
School Board of Advice on Nov. 12, graduated
Bachelor of Medicine and of Surgery at Melb.
University in ’92. She is the middle member of
a sisterly trio of practising doctors.
The Borneo Government turns the philately
craze to account by issuing a new and attractive
postage-stamp design every year. The stamps
that pass through the Borneo post on envelopes
represent only a very small percentage of the num-
ber sold to dealers. And so with other small
states, whose pretty stamps are made principally
to be looked at, not for practical use.
The claim of N.S.W. solicitors to a share
of the judicial appointments—both District and
Supreme Court —is backed by lots of common-
sense, and now that a judge’s order can turn a
solicitor into a barrister, even legal etiquette can
suggest no difficulty. Chief Justice Martin filled
the office of Attorney-General while a solicitor,
and, had he gone straight from that office to the
Bench, it might have been all the better for the
administration of law. Solicitors who have been
appointed judges in other parts of Australia have
left records quite equal to those of barrister-
judges.
Frank Hann, a well-known Northern iden-
tity, reports that while crossing the Northern
Territory he shot, for food, a clean-skin on a run
supposed to be perfectly clean. The animal’s hide
was full of ticks. On mentioning this to the
stockman, Hann tells that the latter admitted he
knew it all the time, but the official statement was
that the run was clean and, therefore, &c., &c.
The Rev. D. F. Barry, late, rector of St-
John’s College, Sydney, was a pleasing figure
about Manly and other suburbs for many years.
His death at Kerang (Vic.) puts finis to his pro-
posed biography of the late Archbishop Vaughan
—a work which he fondly regarded as the magnum
opus of his life. Much of the MSS. has passed
through the hands of members of the Vaughan
family, who have extolled its tone and aim. Barry
and Vaughan were members of the same religious
order.
M.P.—Mighty parasite. J.P.—Junior
Parasite. M.L.C.— Men Like Cain. M.L.A.—
Men Like Abel.
M'Dermott, can it Vie
That you, perchance, have seen
“ Another ” fair as .she !
(The goddess, Mac., we mean)
Our gaze would never feast
On meaner women’s charms
If holy-ordered priest
(lave her into these arms.
Or so we fondly think,
And ever will, although
Your sated eye you wink,
Content to let her go.
A taste of married life—
Erstwhile a blissful dream—
Has made your matchless wife
Uninteresting seem.
“The toy so fiercely sought ” —■
Some cynic b ird has sung—
Grows wear.. mie when caught.
But still our lute is strung
To love’s sweet concert pitch,
And boldly we aver
Earth hath no beauty which
Could win our heart from her !
Pause and consider, Mac.,
Ere yet it be too late,
And try to woo her back
To bless your married state,
Lest one day you deplore
Your present frame of mind,
And kneel in vain before
The Queen of Womankind.

Melb.: “ Met Trenwith to-day. Bit thin-
ner. His shiny little black belltopper has the
mysterious Graham Berryish London polish. His
physique is such that he could take Hancock by
the scruff of the neck, with the right hand, and
Cook with the left and shake them both. So it is
no use their talking of getting into his boots.”
“ Clicquot ” is the modern substitute for
the nectar of the gods, and the gods only drank
nectar because they couldn’t get Clicquot. The
nicest people drink nothing but Clicquot, which
elevates them in the very nicest way. Clicquot,
in fact, is the true philosopher’s drink, trans-
muting this leaden earth at one draught into a
golden Paradise. And there are people who have
never tasted Clicquot ! *
INTERCOLONIAL BAND CONTEST.
The first Intercolonial Band Contest took place
in Sydney Exhibition Building on Nov. 7 and 9th
with great success—bandsmen from all the colo-
nies attending in full regalia. Our own Newtown
boys proved themselves to be the conquerors,
carrying off the £lOO championship and the
Besson and Boosey cups. It is a curious fact to
note that all prize winners (the champions in-
cluded), with few exceptions, had Besson & Co’s
instruments, Boosey & Co. being the next most
successful maker. The contest was well-attended,
and it is to be hoped that this will be the means
of starting many fresh bands. Much attraction
was caused by two fine show-cases from Paling &
Co., exhibiting Besson and Boosey instruments.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Carnage, ridden as Nevvhaven was, would cer-
tainly have run away with the Melb. Cup in which Tar-
coola beat him by half a length. The youngster led all
the way up to the last 50 yards, and for a mile or more
the boy jockey was “pulling his head off” in accordance
with orders. Carnage came in exhausted by his long
struggle with the ill-advised boy on his back, and not a
bit beaten by the pace. He could have done the journey
in faster time had he been allowed to gallop away until
he felt inclined to listen to reason. The continual pulling
represented, without a doubt, a loss of many lengths to
the wayward colt.
* * * 4
S.A. sportsmen, despite the insinuating verdict
of the V.R.C. stewards, decline to believe that David
James, the owner of Broken Hill, has ever been a party to
a horse of his being run cronk. James rarely backs his
horses, and does not depend on the game for a living, as
he is one of the wealthiest men in Australia. When
Auraria won last year’s Cup James was one of the smallest
winners among those “ in the know.”
* * * •
A tout, who holds forth under the name of
“ Ascot ” in Melb. ’Erald, has either committed perjury
or retired from business, probably the former. “In my
notes yesterday,” wrote this luckless oracle, a week before
the Derby, “/ stated that Newhaven was at a false price
for the Derby, and after their appearance 2 to 1 was
freely offered against the son of Oceana.” Then he pro-
ceeded to explain how Newhaven aforesaid was going
from bad to worse in his gallops. The animal had no
chance. “ Ascot,” not content with knocking him out,
with one stroke of a terrible pen, to 2 to 1 for the Derby,
was determined to do the public another service by ex-
posing the hopelessness of the impostor’s Cup-chance, as
follows : “ If Newhaven wins the Melbourne Cup—well, I
won’t eat him, but I’ll give up touting.” There is much
pathos in the career of a man who wears a check suit and
a horse-shoe pin, and rises at 5 a.m. to acquire “ informa-
tion ” which leads to nothing better than that.
* • * •
George Upward, who knows what is a “ good
thing ” at a regatta when he sees one, doesn’t seem to be
impressed by the goodness of “ good things” on the turf.
A lot of horses, remarks G.U., are flogged along at top
speed for the last half of an average racing-journey,
jostling one another as they go, and finally Beelzebub wins
by the length of your umbrella. This lucky animal is the
“ good thing.” The jockey would probably have made
his rush earlier if he hadn’t been blocked, in which case
he might have “ come too soon,” and then any one of
three or four others would have proved a “ good thing ”
instead of Beelzebub.
* * * *
Steadfast, by Nordenfeldt from Nellie Moore,
was sold in Melbourne last week for 42 guineas. J. E.
Brewer was the purchaser, with an idea, seemingly, of
being able to put the dotty black gelding over hurdles.
• * * •
A man’s luck changes every seven years. Bravo
1889, Newhaven 1896. The owner did not see either win.
* » * *
A Northern gravedigger drew the big prize in a
Tatt.’s sweep four years ago. He threw down his spade
and took to horse-backing, and so far he is miles ahead.
His latest was Cremorne and Newhaven (£1000), and he
refused to hedge a copper.
* « * *
One very tempting dream “tip” for the Melb.
Cup came from a Jewish lady, the sister of an experienced
punter, who dreamt that the three winning numbers on
the cards for the treble event—Caulfield Cup, Derby, and
Melbourne Cup—were 4. 1. 4. The first two numbers duly
came off. Cremorne was No. 4 on the card and New-
haven was No. 1, the dreamer and her fair friends backing
the tip each time. The writer hasn’t heard whether Old
Nick persuaded them to dash all their winnings upon No.
4 for the Melbourne Cup. He hopes not. The super-
natural information broke down in its third leg, so to
Bpeak.
* * * *
The saddest (recorded) attempt at picking the
Cup-winner was that of a Melb. ’Erald correspondent,
who wrote the names of prominent Cup horses upon some
ducks’-eggs which were hatched shortly before the event.
“ The strange part,” he said, “is that the idea was sug-
gested with a view to finding a dead-bird, and one died
almost as soon as it came out of the shell. This dead-bird
was ‘ Devon.’ ” The Bulletin is haunted by a brain-pic-
ture of the infatuated poultry-breeder squatting for hours
beside the maternal bird that he might raise it from the
warm eggs every time his keen ear detected the crack of a
shell, with a view to the discovery of a dying duckling.
What a cranky vigil!
* * • *
Joshua Rose, a Hawkesbury native, died last
week at Warialda (N.S.W.), aged 70. For 50 years he was
connected with horse-racing, and rode in races when 68
years of age.
* * * *
The A.J.C. Summer Cup and Tatt.’s Cup have
closed with 36 entries apiece, mostly second-raters. Long-
distance racing isn’t popular at midsummer.
* * * *
The popular Mr. Rowley Pickering (“ Nemo,”
of Sydney Mail), who has been 28 years on the Sydney
press, and for 26 years of that time in the S.M. Herald
office, has resigned his position with a view to ruralising
for a year or two. Rowley takes with him into retirement
the best wishes of all.
• * • *
The other day secretary Byron Moore had a few
sensible remarks to make concerning the wail of the
“ flattite ” who considers himself unfairly treated at
Flemiugton races in not being supplied with much more
than a full view of the sport. The flattite, as B.M. might
have pointed out, is an uninvited dead-head reviling the
management for not feeling anxious about his well-being.
On the other side of the course people buy admission to
the Stand or Hill, and their interests are studied on sound
commercial principles. They provide the show. Visitors
to the Flat don’t rank as patrons of the entertainment,
and have no claim to the smallest consideration. If they
can afford the price of a reserved place they ought to
contribute it, if they can’t stand the expense they have
no business to be. at Flemington at all. Horse-racing is
not a healthy game for impecunious mortals. Yet the
non-productive army of free passers has devoured a
small percentage of the V.R.C. profits. Deadheads have
a starting-board which gives them as much information
as it gives to folks who pay the piper. Also, they are
furnished with water-taps and other accommodation
which public decency demands,, and which, in all other
public places, is charged to the public at large. Here it
is enjoyed by one section only of the public at the cost of
a club which the other section supports.
Touching the complaint that officials are sent
around the Flat in a cart to gather up any boxes devoted
to the elevation of bookmakers, Byron Moore explains
that the same thing would be done in the reserve if it
were necessary. Bookmakers perched on boxes are con-
trary to the Act anywhere, whilst bookmakers standing
on high in order that they may the more easily capture
the coin of punters who haven’t spared a red cent to the
show, are adding insult to illegality. It is time for the
wail of the flattite to cease. Flemington racecourse was
a bit of public land granted to the V.R.C. on the same
terms as the Melb. Cricket Club holds its reserve. At a
cricket-match there isn’t any free standing space ; at a
Cup race there is. In the one case the right to grumble
seems to be admitted along with the non-producers. In
the other case complaints are not heard, for the reason
that the would-be complainants have not where to lay
their dead-heads.
* * • *
Yet another St. Simon sire for N.S.W. The
Dangars have bought for their Neotsfield Stud the
unraced Positano (St. Simon—Ponza). Ponza is by
Springfield from Napoli, by Macaroni from Sunshine, by
Thormanby—a pedigree bristling with the best running
blood extant.

Anti-cyclist: “As soon as I can afford to buy a
bike, I shall buy a horse.”
As a good Orangeman, Stanbury should have
won the Thames waterman’s prize—-Dogget’s Coat and

* * * *
Swimmer G. Cavill seems to have struck a happy
hunting-ground in American waters. His ’Frisco trip did
not eventuate in anything noteworthy ; he had only one
quarter-mile race, and a showman swim across the Golden
Gate. Cavill returns to America in April to prosecute a
considerable programme. He is a good long-distance
swimmer, and whether he is the best long-distance swim-
mer in Australia is a question which this country would
rather concede than put to the test. This country can’t
stand long-distance swimming, or 25-mile hicycle races, or
a week’s club-swinging, or a long sermon, or any other
thing which from its nature is liable to merge itself into
eternity.
* * * *
O. W. Bennett, of Aus. cycling fame, is back
from England, his birthplace. He says he is sorry he ever
visited the world’s retail shop again : “ It’s the home of
drizzle, dreariness, and dirt; thank God for the Australian
sunshine.”
He saw the trio—Megson, Payne, and Lewis —
cycle themselves into public ridicule. The climate de-
pressed them to death, says Bennett. “ One day when
the sun by some strange caprice shone really warm, the
three met Michael, the Welsh wonder, on the road ; and
they gave Michael all he wanted. But when it came to
racing on the track they failed pitifully. I saw them and
I prayed for the earth to open and swallow me. I couldn’t
have imagined them showing with such utter feebleness.
It’s the climate, the English climate ; after Australia it’s a
plague.
“ Compared with the circumstances of English
cycling, our racing here is as pure as a lily. The riders
are above our men in social status, but the jobbery and
trickery winked at upon the tracks would make the
wickedest Aus. cyclist tremble. The big monopolists who
run pacing teams can make this or that man win at plea-
sure. The pacing there is the art of cycling arts. A man
well-paced is picked up by quad after quad without a jar,
without a tremor, without a suggestion of variation in
gait, and he is drawn along at a swiftly uniform pace
which will almost pull him to victory in spite of himself.
But if they don’t want a man to win they’ll pace him
atrociously ; they’ll slow down, they’ll jump away, they’ll
rush in a sprint and leave him broken up and stone-blind
with exertion. Given our men here fit and well, and the
Englishmen fit and well, I think our fellows would come
out of the contest with great credit.”
* * » *
The lily-white amateurs of Melb. managed last
Saturday’s meeting as badly as usual, with the result that
the programme finished about an hour late. The attend-
ance was chiefly confined to the stand and the reserve,
one side of the ground consisting of a beautiful green
slope unbroken by a single figure. Wally Kerr seemed
all out of condition, judging by the three races he started
in, for he was beaten fairly and squarely each time. Gil-
lespie beat him by a wheel in the Mile Championship,
after leading for the last lap with Kerr making desperate
efforts to get by. Berry, in the Half-mile Handicap, was
passed by the Sydney crack 100yds. from home, but came
up on the outside and finished a foot ahead, after a great
sprint. Both fell on passing the post, and both, with
more pluck than sense, came up to the post in the Five-
mile Championship. Here Scott, a red-headed youngster,
went out half-a-lap from home, and finished with Berry,
Kerr, and Gillespie following, with about 6in. between
each.
On his Saturday’s riding, Archer Berry—son of
the Vic. Speaker—shows himself amongst the most
promising of this season’s batch of Vic. cyclists. He starts
a trifle late—he is 23 now— but possesses both sprint and
stamina.
One of last Saturday’s cycling amateurs created
great excitement in the dressing-room by producing a
razor and carefully shaving his legs before appearing on
the track 1 Bilmartin watched the operation with a grin
that was worth pounds to see. The same competitor an-
nounced loudly that that occasion would be his last ap-
pearance on the racing-track, as he did not think it right
“ for a gentleman amateur to compete before people who
had paid for admission.”
That great athlete, Roseingrave, the Irish cham-
pion (where is O’lsaac, by the way ?), got through without
a defeat, annexing the pole jump, the 120yds. hurdle, the
long jump, and the high-jump championships without
much trouble. Next to Rosey, the most prominent
athletic figure was that of C. H. Watson, a powerfully-
built member of the Melbournians, who secured the 100
and 200 yd. championships, besides running second to the
Hibernian’s long jump of 22ft. Bin. with one of 21ft. lin.
“Jimmy” Doyle, holder of the Australian high-jump re-
cord, seconded to Roseingrave in that event, being cap-
able of only sft. 6jin. as against his best of sft. lOin.
The programme, which was the only well-
arranged part of the whole show, showed the Vic. 100yds.
record as 9 9-10 sec. Nine-tenths of a second is cutting
things a trifle fine.
A particularly silly arrangement was reckon-
ing the length of the long jump from the nearer side of a
white plank sunk in the earth as a mark. This caused
the competitors to take off from the hard board with their
spiked shoes or else lose six inches from their jump. All
but Roseingrave chose the latter course. Bv the way,
Roseingrave, unlike other athletes, wears spikes on the
heel as well as on the sole of his foot.
George Turner, the Vic. Premier’s son, trained
hard for the sports until he heard Wally Kerr was going
to start. Then he decided that he couldn’t strike form,
and stood off on Saturday. Now he’s wild enough to
throw eggs at himself.
“ Hooroo for Ireland ! ” yelled an excited
Milesian in the grand-stand on Roseingrave’s fourth win
“ Fetch out yer blanky Orangemin ter bate thot.”

been received. The short season he put in here panned
out £2BO.
* « * *
Barrister Coldham, who opposed Premier Turner
in the Fatman interest at last St. Kilda election, is one of
the principals in the movement to prevent the amateur
cyclists amalgamating with Vic. League of Wheelmen, and
one of those who would support a split of the former
should such a consummation be effected. Yet this same
Mr. Ooldham, who is such a fervent barracker for “ purity
of the sport” and lily-white amateurism, puts off his prin-
ciples in his leisure moments to shoot pigeons for cash
sweepstakes. Which somehow recalls (we have long hun-
gered for a chance to lug it in) the story of the eminent
counsel who, finding a poor street-walker sitting on the
doorstep of his chambers, said, “My good woman you
have no right here.” “No,” replied the frail one, “I
have no right; lam only a wigless prostitute.”
A fielder at the Brassey Cycle Sports laid a level
half-sov. against Parsons, and refused to pay on losing;
he was run in, but the P.M. said it wasn't welshing as he
did not bolt, and advised prosecutor that he had only a
civil remedy. Prosecutor met the fielder outside and
with a big stick recovered the 10s.

Ed. Bulletin, —No doubt Bilmartin has been

* * * *
Scorching is doomed in Austria. Trying a
charge of reckless riding, the Bench ruled that “ the
cyclist must always keep his machine under control and
possess such skill that in a moment of danger he can in-
stantly bring it to a standstill or dismount.” Further,”
the cyclist who lifts both hands from the handlebar
thereby commits a police offence.”
* * * *
Parsons is not yet 21 years old. He accounts
for his present lack of form by explaining that his legs
are troubled with a rheumatic affection, and he has also
been suffering from dysentery.
* * • *
Sydney Cricket Ground for maintenance and
improvements the last 20 years has cost £90,000. It is
now about the finest ground in the world.
* * » *
When a certain team of Australian athletes left
for the old world the management had much trouble in
getting one member away from his too fiery affinity.
Ultimately he was persuaded to forswear roses and rap-
tures for duty and dumb-bells, but on touching a seaport
500 miles from his starting-point, the steamer was met by
the lady, who had meanwhile travelled overland. Another
protracted argument ensued, but the management scored
again, and the passionate damsel was despatched home-
ward. No sooner had the amorous Australian touched
foreign land than he cabled for the apple of his eye to
join him. Did she? Well, just look at his record-sheet
for answer.

« * * *
Gus Kearney, the erst JEssendon footballer,
carried off Vic. tennis championship from holder Dunlop
last Saturday practically by one stroke. Each had won
two sets, and when 22 sets of the final had been played,
Dunlop had scored 40 to Kearney’s 30. Then a soft ball
came apparently right into his hands. But as he rose to
strike, it grazed the top of the net and broke sidewavs,
giving his opponent the advantage. At the 28th game
Kearney secured the necessary points and the champion-
ship. Miss Phoebe Howitt, a daughter of Dr. Howitt, won
the Ladies’ Singles, and with her sister the Doubles. Each
of these wins was for the second time, giving the agile
girls final possession of a couple of massive silver trophies.

player and the idol of the small-fry barrackers, may re-
move to Sydney, at Christmas, owing to the troubles at
Adelaide Hospital, where he is a student.
* * * »
A Bulletin correspondent:—lf you ever have
the christening of a racehorse, don’t call him “ Robbo ”
or any name commencing with “R.” It is a luckless
initial. No winner of a Melb. Cup, nor sire of a winner,
bore it; neither is it in the list of Maribyrnong Plate or
Caulfield Cup winners. It only appears in the Vic. Derby
twice, borne by Robin Hood and Rufus. The former was
drowned in the City of Melbourne wreck, and the latter
seems never after to have been heard of. Now for a few
good and favorite horses who carried this unlucky initial:
Richmond, sire of many a fine animal, and himself pluck
to the hoofs, was fluked out of a Melb. Cup by Wollomai.
Robinson Crusoe, in his short career, deserved better
than he got. What a pot boiled over when Ruenalf failed
in the Cup a couple of years ago ! Why dilate on the
luckless Richelieu ? —and Resolute seems equally unlucky
to-day. Englishmen will remember how Bend Or beat
the favorite and better horse Robert the Devil in the
English Derby. Redleap is the exception to prove the rule.

* » * •
In some parts of the North Island of Maoriland
the Maoris have annual races, where oats are received as
entries and disbursed as stakes. Here is a copy of the
rules posted at one of these “oats meetings” (held at
Havelock, near Napier) (1) This race for horses that
have never won a race excee ling £2 and up to £lOO. (2)
If two horse come to deadhead, the stake will pay out
to two of them and the dividend to the two. (3) If a man
protess the first horse past the post, the dividend will pay
to the first horse that past the post. (4) We allowed any
jockey to ride at this race, a man cannot protess. (5)
There are 6 hurdle at this race. (6) The club don’t allow
any bookmaker on the ground. (7) The race will start at
10 o’clok. (8) We do not allowed any horseman to go on
the ground.
* * * *
On a Sydney suburban course, one of the very
“ smartest ” trainers called the handicapper aside and
asked him particularly “to watch the running of his
horse, which had only been in hand three weeks.” Now,
before starting for the course, the horse had got fairly
stuffed with bran—probably wandered from his box to
the feed-room—and the owner’s instructions to jockey
were : “ Make every post a winning one.” During the
running of the race owner and handicapper stood together.
The bran seemed rather to agree with the gee, and he
rounded into the straight with a strong lead, the handi-
capper remarking to the owner, “ I’m watching him care-
fully, old man! ” And as the horse finished, with his
field nearly distanced, the owner almost fell down dead
as the handicapper, putting his glasses away with
thoughtful mien, shook him heartily by the hand with
the remark—“ If that’s three weeks’ work, in the name of
thunder what’ll he be like in six ! ”

Notes and Queries explores the longevity ol
the 16 rowers taking part in the Oxford and Cambridge
boat-race, 1829. Their average life was over 69 years.
* * » «
At Brixton prison, Eng., there was a mutiny
among the prisoners, because they were not put on the
treadmill. They argued that they were deprived of theii
regular bicycle exercise, and, if they couldn’t keep up
their practice on the stepper, they would be quite out of
fashion by the time they got out of gaol.
* * * *
The best and handiest means to locate a tyre-
puncture is a bit of common soap carried in the pocket.
Water is not always available in quantity to fill the tyre
and locate by leakage, but if the tyre is rubbed with soap
and squeezed, a soap-bubble at once forms where the
slightest puncture is, and repair is easy as falling off «
roof.
* * * *
The science of snatching tickets from winnen
as they go to get paid by the cash fielders is getting quite
acclimatised on Australian racecourses. At one course s
professional spieler is said to be in league with a couple ol
welshers, whose duty it is to grab the winning tickets
from the mugs who want their money, and clear out in
the crowd. If the mug makes any outcry, a robbery-
with-assault man is kept in reserve to bash him on the
jaw.
* * ♦ *
“ Every day,” writes a bike-expert to The Bul-
letin, “cyclists are seen tugging at the handles as
though they were the reins of a run-away war-horse,
to get out of the way of trams and cabs. Nearly all lady
riders take several shots at turning a corner. To swing
the machine round with easy grace, all that is needed is
to incline the body to and press the handle on the side to
which it is desired to turn, and keep the pedals going all
the time—in the city one’s life often depends upon good
steering.”

The principal business of the Anglican Assem-
bly, held on Tuesday last (17th), at Melb., was the election
of “ the Panel of Triers.” It’s a good thing that such a
body isn’t a necessary adjunct to a race-meeting, for it
would often be mighty hard to raise a quorum.

Mr. C. W. Bennett says that Mr. Kilburn Edge,
late of Edge and Edge, Sydney, has been appointed
general manager in Australia for the Austral Cycle Co.,
vice Mr. E. W. Rudd, resigned. Mr. Kilburn Edge is
brother to Mr. S. F. Edge, of Dunlop Tyre Co. and John
Griffiths Cycle Co-operation.

Every day it becomes more apparent that
when Nelson leaves the political arena, Tozer will
grasp the reins in Bananaland. Old Pricklypear
defers openly to the astute Hon. Secretary, where-
at the other aspirant, Byrnes, chafes, and girds up
his loins for the coming fray.

Naif-Yearly Report of tbe PirectorS,
With the Accounts for THE HALF-YEAR to 13th APRIL, 1896. Presented to the Proprietors at
the Half-yearly General meeting, held on THURSDAY, Ist OCTOBER, 1896.
REPORT.
The Directors submit to the Proprietors the balance-sheet as at 13th April last, with the profit
account for the half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and
colonial rates and taxes, and for all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the half-year amounted
to £40,568 10s. The addition of £11,653 14s. 5d., brought forward from the previous half-year, gives a
disposable balance of £52,222 4s. 5d., of which the dividend declared will absorb £40,000, leaving
£12,222 4s. sd. to be carried forward to next account.
Business generally in the colonies during the half-year has shown signs of improvement, and a
slightly better demand for property is reported. The coming season, too, promises to be more favor-
able for squatters and agriculturists. The “ tick ” pest amongst cattle is causing anxiety in some
districts of Queensland, but the Government are taking active measures to isolate the infection.
The advance in the value of wool, mentioned in the last report, has not been maintained, "and the
frozen meat trade still continues depressed, though some improvement in the latter has recently taken
place.
The bank’s business, as is indicated by the figures of the balance-sheet, shows an increase upon
that of the preceding half-year, though, unfortunately, the low rates prevailing prevented a corres-
ponding improvement in the profit.
The dividend declared is at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum, or £1 per share for the half-year
and will be payable, free of income tax, in London and in the colonies, on the 2nd October. '
4 Threadneedle-street, London, 24th September, 1896.
MARTIN R. SMITH, Chairman.

London, September 14, 1896.
Ed. Bulletin, —The absurd bicycle craze is a
Striking instance of the extent to which the unthinking
multitude can be “kidded” into following a fashionable
fad. As an exercise, cycling is manifestly injurious,
because, while it throws a severe strain upon vital organs,
it cramps and distresses them, preventing their free and
natural action. Running, swimming, boxing, club-
swinging, dumb-bells, cricket and tennis are all incom-
parably superior, for the simple reason that the person
indulging in any one of these carries his body in nature’s
attitude, thus allowing his vital organs the free and
healthful play demanded by nature. If a man cycles
with upright attitude, then he gets off at every hill, and
walking up it counteracts the injurious effects of the
game. To ride up-hill the cyclist must bow his neck and
plug. On plain road the cycle (barring fresh-air con-
siderations) gives no better exercise than the sewing-
machine, and no one regards the sewing-machine as an
unmixed blessing. The man who has begun cycling as a
child, and taken no other form of exercise, will not have
the leg-development of the runner or swimmer, while his
chest will be the flat chest of a tape-worm. The fact that
a few smaller lights of the medical profession patronise the
cycle, shows that several little doctors are as big fools as
the rest of cycling mankind ; some doctors play football
or ride steeplechases, but their doing so does not show
that football or steeplechasing are reasonably free from
chance of accident or death. The great bulk of the
cyclists who say that the game does them good, are over-
grown children exhilarated with a new toy. Others who
praise it are people who never had a sweat or a bath until
cycling came along with its drenching of sweat and mud,
necessitating much soap and water. When the heated
imagination of cycle-mad humanity cools down to the
conditions of sanity, the cyclf enthusiast will be lodged
m a lunatic asylum, and there, under the influence of
cycling’s horrible vibratory disease, he will jabber eternally
to himself about the virtues of the wheel. — Key.
• • * •
The wheel is making a system for itself in Aus-
tralia. The Vic. Wheelmen’s League has arranged with
all Vic. hotels for special rates. The N.S.W. Cycling
Tourists’ Union is fixing the same thing in its pro-
vince. N.S.W. has the Wheelman paper; Vic. has the
Australian Cyclist. Cash prizes are increasing in num-
ber and amount. More riders are being retained, and
riders’ salaries are being raised. The Wheelmen’s Leagues
are all prosperous or have only themselves to blame where
they are not so. One recent move of N.S.W. League was
to make Percy Hunter general secretary at £3 per week, i
Hunter also has a share in Sydney Wheelman.

An Italian professor finds a decoction of
eucalyptus-leaves a powerfully antidotal stomach-
injection in strychnine poisoning cases.
In far Northern S.A. sheep have been
trying to keep alive by eating dead rabbits. One
squatter has lost 26,000, and another (Mortlock,
M.P.) 35,000 during the drought.
A Florida surgeon finds that the bile of a
rattlesnake applied freely to a bite of that snake,
and poured into an incision at the site, not only
guards against death, but actually prevents swel-
ling and other symptoms. The remedy, in view
of antitoxin successes lately, seems far from un-
likely. It is certainly worth investigating in
snake-infested Australia.
Melb. : ‘ ‘ All ecclesiasticism nowadays must
be allied with show business, from the Synagogue,
which calleth in the aid of Fitzgerald’s circus
unto the very Hallelujah rakers on the muck-heap
of sin. Bills in my suburb notify: ‘ Salvation
Army. The Musical Lilliputians. Exhibition of
Scarf and Flag Drill, Dumb-bell and Club Exer-
cises, as Taught in Our Band of Love.’ More
innocuous, anyhow, than the Band of Love teach-
ing in the ante-room of St. Kilda Barracks.”
On the island of Panaieti, near the
coast of British New Guinea, the natives bury
their dead under the earthen floor of their huts.
Phew ! Traders and missionaries are not neces-
sarily anthropologists, or perhaps they would ex-
plain a custom also common on the same island.
A corpse is allowed to rest in its grave for a year
only. Then the duty of exhuming “ the dust-not-
quite-to-dust ” falls to the lot of some old woman,
who scrapes the bones carefully and well oils them,
after which they are hung up for three months in
the roof to smoke. The final rite of burial consists
in securely fastening up the brown skeleton in a
grass mat and hiding it away underground in some
unknown and remote bush spot.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
X.Z. : “Have animals humor ? lately asked
an English magazine. Mr. Walter Gibson, of
Glenholm, Dromana, Vic., kept for several years
a tame kangaroo as a children’s pet, which ran
loose on his estate. It’s great joy was to quickly
slip in among the cows, grip the tail of one with
its forepaws, and, with a vigorous kick with its
powerful hind leg, start that bewildered cow on a
mad race round the paddocks, using the tail as
reins. ‘Joey’ was shot one day by a Melb.
cockney sport who mistook him for a warrigal
kangaroo. Seen to do it by a local resident, the
shootist got a big licking, and had to carry the
corpse home on his back.”

The moral of a recently well-advertised-
in-advance Sydney operation: Keep quiet until
you have got the bullet out.
Rev. P. Y. Dwyer, president of W. Mait-
land R.C. College, and son of Public-school In-
spector Dwyer, of Sydney, is the nominee of the
bishops for the Catholic assistant-bishopric of
Maitland, and will be the first Australian-born
Catholic bishop. He is 38 years of age.
Camille Flammarion, the French astrono-
mer, has found that plants grown under a cover-
ing of red glass increase to nearly 16 times their
original size in the course of 12 weeks, whereas the
same plants under blue glass remain stationary.
Possibly this law holds good with regard to human
beings. If so many apparent anomalies are cap-
able of easy explanation. For instance, the heads
of some eminent public men must have grown to
their present size under red glass—and their brains
under blue.

A TERRIBLE EXPERIENCE
OP A SNOW STORM IN
AFRICA^
CHIEF CONSTABLE HUDSON COULD
NOT GET SHELTER.
A terrible snow storm occurred awhile ago in
Griqualand East, South Africa. Mr. E. W.
Hudson, Chief Constable of Tsolo, in a letter
dated 15th July, writes as follows concerning it: —
“ Last winter, whilst on duty, I got caught in a
heavy snow storm in the mountains and could not
get shelter of any kind. The result was that I
got rheumatics all down my left side. I now
think it my duty to thank you for the benefit I
have derived from Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. The
pain in my shoulder and hip was simply awful. I
could not move, nor could I take a long breath
without pain. My duty is mostly on horseback,
and I cannot describe what I suffered at times
when I had to go on duty. I became so low, both
in mind and body, that I was afraid I should have
to give in. I tried all sorts of remedies, embroca-
tions, etc. Some relieved me for a time, but
nothing permanent. Some of my friends recom-
mended me to try Dr. William’s Pink Pills. I
never did believe in patent medicines, and it was
some time before I took the advice, which I regret
now, as I might have been saved months of suffer-
ing had I listened earlier. I had an idea that
these medicines act like a spur on a jaded horse,
livening him up for a bit, and then leaving him
sorrier than ever. But this was not the case with
Dr. William’s Pink Pills. I sent for some, and

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints &A, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DU ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities —a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,

The English tobacco firm of Wills and Co.
(owner of the Birdseye and Three Castles brands)
has paid to British Govt, as much as =£Boo,ooo
duty in one year, and .£lOO,OOO in a day. It pays
up to one-twelfth of the whole tobacco revenue.
Queensland bacteriologist Pound has hopes
of coping with the rabbit plague. He intends to
adopt the plan by which M. Pasteur cleared out
the bunnies on Madame Pommery’s estates in
Prance. Australia, however, has already dozens
of plans which succeed admirably on small estates.
It’s when the estate measures a million square
miles that the trouble commences.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
A hapless leper was discovered the other
day at Cairns. - Brisbane officials wired to Cairns,
ordering the suffering man to be despatched, per
Pearless, back to the islands, along with 30 other
islanders. Boat refused passenger, whereupon
the Kanaka took up lodgings in an old boiler, but
was expelled by police. Camped next under an old
fig-tree—expelled again. Resorted then to his
fellow Kanakas, who expelled him once more. At
present, he is probably disseminating leprous
virus among the aboriginals, or dropping a section
of a lumpy lobe into the soup at some fresh pub.
which is indulging in the common bush-luxury of
a cheap cook.
The valiant baker of a Sydney suburb,
lately mulct in sundry five shillingses for
carrying around light-weight bread in a basket,
holding still that the light-weight was accidentally
brought about, threatens to startle the court if
ever taken there on a similar charge. The
“ startle ” is to be the resurrection of George IV.
as a legal barrier. It appears that the George IV.
Act, dealing with bakers, Specifies that no travel-
ling baker need carry scales or otherwise profess
to weigh unless his barrow or cart is drawn by a
beast of burden. This Act was tinkered in the
time of William IV., but the specified clause was
allowed to stand, and in that state the law has
come into force in N.S.W. Can, then, a baker
who hawks with a basket be called a “ beast of
burden ”? and if not, is not the knight of the
paper-hat as fairly entitled to resurrect the unre-
pealed George IV. on a flour-and-water question
as was N:S. W. Government a few years ago to
resurrect the unrepealed George IV. on a con-
spiracy question ? ”
Mr. Arthur Griffith wishes to notify intend-
ing patentees that he has commenced practice as a
Patents and Trade Marks Agent at 39 Castlereagh-
street, Sydney. He has entered into arrange-
ments with leading agents in Europe, America,
Australasia, and South Africa, so that inventors
may rely upon having their interests protected.
Mr. Griffith’s charges will, in all cases, be so
moderate as to place a patent within the reach of
the poorest inventor. For £2O, exclusive of the
cost of plans, Mr. Griffith will obtain Provisional
Protection for any invention in the seven Austra-
lasian colonies and Great Britain.*
A Russian woman, who was in the habit
of swallowing everything she could get hold of,
when emptied the other day by the surgeons
yielded 37 different articles, including a fork,
several inches of iron bar, two spoons, a needle, a
piece of lace, two nails, four bits of glass, eight
buttons, a key, &o. On recovery, she toured
along with her recent contents, and made enough
in a few months to retire on a competency for life.
Picturesque Smellbourne as portrayed in a
letter to the Argus -
Can any city in the world that aspires to those civilising
influences which a corporate council is supposed to bestow,
exhibit a more purely healthy picture than the lumbering
scavenger’s cart of Melbourne, replete to overflowing
with the garbage of a hundred back yards, pursuing its
aromatic course through the public streets at 12 noon on
a tropical summer’s day ?

A Bulletin man who recently did a walk-
ing tour from Sydney to Mudgee struck one not-
able fact. He counted over 100 more or less dead-
beat swagmen on the road, and all but five of them
were heading towards Sydney. The great object
of almost all Government relief works is to gather
up the Sydney unemployed—because it is the
Sydney unemployed who are in evidence at the
Statue and in deputations to the Premier and in
processions along Macquarie-street, and who are a
beastly nuisance generally—and shunt them out
West on to scrub-cutting and forest-thinning and
irrigation jobs. The procession one meets on the
roads heading Sydney-wards consists largely of
these derelicts walking back. There is a lot of
sound wisdom in the Bogan scrub-cutting philo-
sophy. It takes the unemployed a long time to get
there, and, even if the job doesn’t amount to much,
he generally stays a little while, and then, even if
his boots hold out, it takes him two or three
months to walk back, and the city is free of him in
the meantime. A really enterprising policy of
sending all the unemployed to dig a stupendous
dam at Broken Hill would be the best thing of all,
however. When the job gave out most of them,
rather than face the great plain track to Cobar,
would head for Adelaide, and be permanently
stranded there.

A Criterion of Cleverness .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was about 7 a.m. when I strolled over
from the office of the Quarran-Yarran Times
—of which influential organ I was then editor—to
have a yarn with Pooler, the landlord of the
Royal Hotel. We had been chatting for a few
minutes when old Dr. Miggs, looking, as he always
did at that hour of the morning, very seedy and
very shaky, dropped in for his usual restorer-
half a tumbler of Three Star with an egg beaten
up in it. After a brief “ Good morning, doctor,
I "turned my back on him and looked out of t he
window. It would have been cruel to watch the
old man taking, with his hand and head shaking
together, his first drink for the day. But after he
had swallowed it he was ready for conversation.
“ How did Mrs. get through her trouble .
enquired Pooler. ,„ ~ ,
“ Splendidly, sir, splendidly,” was the reply;
“ she was in my hands ! ” ~
There was, of course, nothing more to be said
in the way of interrogation after that. But Dr.
Miggs, growing more affable as the brandy got its
work in, volunteered the information that Mr.
was the father of another fine boy. Then
he (the doctor) began to grumble after his cus-
tomary fashion. ,
“ Why the devil,” he said, didn’t they send
for young Martin instead of rousing me up in the
middle of the night ? ” .
As there was no answer forthcoming he supplied
one himself. .... , ~ ,
“ Because, sir,” he exclaimed, they don t put
any faith in these young upstarts, with their new-
fangled theories and their scientific airs, who pre-
tend to know more than men old enough to bo
their grandfathers. That’s why they didn’t send
for young Martin. ”
The old man, after delivering this statement
with a triumphant chuckle, left us and went on
1 “'isn’t he a wonder!” said Pooler. “Why,
last night, when came riding into the town-
ship hell-for-leather to fetch him to the missus, he
was in here more’n half-drunk. I straightened
him up as well as I could by pumping water on
his head and soda down his throat till he was fit
for his business And see how he pulled it
through ! ” ,
“ But, confound it, Pooler, he wasn’t sober when
he came in here this morning ! ”
“Well, of course, I don’t mean to say he was
quite sober when he left last night. But he was
sober enough to know what he was about and
what more would you want ? ”
I said that if I had been in Mr. ’s place I
should have wanted a great deal more, and would
have seen that I got it, too.
Pooler laughed a trifle scornfully. You
haven’t been long enough in Quarran-Yarran to
know the ways of the people,” he said, “or you
wouldn’t talk in that style. Why, Mr. , if
you paid him, wouldn’t have anybody but old
Si to attend either himself or his missus,
er would his missus.”
“ But, good God, man ! ” I exclaimed, in a land
of rage, “you don’t mean to tell me that you
seriously believe that a man who, according to your
own story and to what I know myself, is always
more or less drunk, and sometimes trembling on
the verge of D.T.’s, is the sort of person to be
entrusted with the issues of life and death . It
you do you must be mad.”
“ Mad or not mad,” replied Pooler, flicking the
dust off his bottles with an apron, “ I mean to say
that Ido believe in old Dr. Miggs. He drinks
like a fish, I know, but he’s damned clever. And,
what’s more to the point, he makes no secret Qt His
drinking. He could do it at home on the quiet it
he liked, but he doesn’t like, and everybody thinks
all the more of him for it.”
Now Pooler was one of the few intelligent men
in Quarran-Yarran. If this were his settled con-
viction, what could be expected from the rest of
the people ?
I have noticed that m the city, when a ravorite
medical man, with a weakness for the bottle, is
referred to, the formula is—“ He’s damned clever,
but he drinks like a fish. ”
In the bush it is put in the converse way—
“He drinks like a fish, but (meaning therefore)
he’s damned clever.”
* • * *
Later in the day I was talking with Dr.
Martin, who had come to Quarran-Yarran some
months before in the hope of being able to estab-
lish a modest practice in that old-fashioned bush
village. After relating the conversation I had
had with Pooler that morning, I said (being- on
somewhat familiar terms with him): “ Now look
here, doctor, I want you to tell me candidly,
leaving aside any prejudice you may have against
him, because the people here are pig-headed
enough to prefer him to you with all your up-to-
date medical knowledge—l want you to tell me
what you think of his real skill as a medical man.
“Well,” replied Dr. Martin in a judicial tone,
“ speaking candidly, he works on principles that
were shown to be idiotic about a century ago.
Why, the damned old mountebank actually still
believes in bleeding for fever ! At the same time
I think he is quite good enough for the patients.”
We had some more talk on the subject, and
then, as I was going, Dr. Martin said: “I see
that I shall have to leave this place. It’s too late
now for me to try to work up a reputation as a
drunkard, but if I should ever make another
start in a bush township, you can bet your life I
shall make my first appearance there in an
advanced stage of the horrors. ”
CREEVE ROE.
Bayley, of Bayley’s Reward, died at
Avenel, Vie., of congestion of the liver, aged 31.
He leaves a widow ; no family. Seymour Tele-
graph gives an interesting account of his career: —
Bayley was a Vic. native, and went prospecting in
N.Q. when only 16. He battled round the Towers,
Ilughenden, Normanton, Croydon, and the Palmer. He
landed in W.A. with 305., tramped to Southern Cross and
earned a little money, which he lost in the Bunbury tin-
mines. Then he walked back to Roebourne, and, with a
mate, walked barefooted to the Ashburton, 280 miles, in
nine days. He did well, went to the Murchison and did
better, and had a trip home to Vic. Then he returned to
W.A., reached the Coolgardie district, and with Ford,
dropped on the Reward after a good many months’ un-
profitable hunting.
They had pegged out a prospecting area when Hannan
and Ready, two other well-known prospectors, appeared
on the scene. Bayley and Ford did not relish being dis-
turbed, and at this Bayley, with characteristic cuteness,
showed Hannan a few fair specimens of gold and earnestly
advised him that it was good enough for him (Hannan) to
stay and try the show. Hannan took the bait so readily
that for fear of himself being tracked to a good show of
his own he struck camp and moved off with all haste the
same night. Bayley and Ford were mad with delight to
find in the morning no trace of their visitors. Some few
days after this, however, an accident occurred
to the pair but for which the find would not
have been made public for at least another two
months. A dingo stole all the bacon fat they had in
camp. Their only means of subsistence was on tinned
meats and damper, and great store was set by their bacon.
There was literally nothing to shoot, and scarcely a living
thing except lizards and aborigines to be seen. It was a
case of a trip of 140 miles into Southern Cross (the nearest
town) for stores, so off they went. On their way back
they again met Hannan and Ready on Hunt s survey
track. Hannan and Ready, in turn meeting another
party, were able to tell them, in answer to their questions,
that Bayley was on ahead. These men proved to be
trackers from Southern Cross, sent out by a small ss.
syndicate for the express purpose of tracing Bayley, who,
from his continuous spell in the district and his occa-
sional call for stores, the astute syndicate reckoned must
be on something surprisingly rich.
Owing to Hannan and Ready’s timely guidance the
trackers found out the show. Bayley and Ford showed
their prospecting area duly pegged and warned the men
off the ground. But it was of no use, and it was found
that after digging a trench as near as possible to the
boundary as a blind, these men had prospected on the
area with so much success that they had exposed the cap
of the magnificent reef from which so much was after-
wards won. They were forced to disgorge 200 oz. admitted
to have been taken from this find, and no doubt this was
not the whole. It was now high time for Bayley and
Ford to set to work, and, leaving Ford behind, Bayley
journeyed into Southern Cross, reported the find, and
took out a lease. So rich was the cap of the reef where
exposed that the first stroke of the pick by Ford dislodged
a lump of ore 70oz. in weight containing 40oz. of pure
gold, and they cut out with a tomahawk a piece contain-
ing 140 oz. Afterwards 500 oz. was dollied out in three
days, and the 6cwt. of tailings, which already had
yielded 17500 z. of pure gold, when treated gave
another 2780 z. Bayley and Ford’s troubles now began
in earnest, as, besides having to continally risk
their lives to save the gold in their possession from
theft, they had to keep a very close and constant watch on
the claim. Also, they had the pleasure of hearing of
rushes planned on every hand. At this stage the utter
fearlessness of Bayley stood him in good stead. One
morning Bayley, returning from a short distance, found
that Ford had been unable to prevent a score of men from
assembling on the ground. Bavlev was wrath and ex-
postulated with the men to some good purpose, but the
ringleader, a big burly man, was immovable until Bayley,
stripping to the waist, offered to fight him for 50ft. on the
line of reef as a stake. The fellow at first was eager for
the fray, but eventually withdrew, whereupon several
others began to mutter their willingness. Bayley saw
trouble in store on these terms, and, quickly producing
two revolvers, he declared that he would shoot down like
a dog the first man who moved a step nearer.
The next episode was a jumping case in which the prin-
cipal actor admitted when under examination by Warden
Finnerty that his employers were a lot of the “leading”
men of Perth. The principal witness was one of the
original trackers, but under cross-examination his evi-
dence broke down and Bayley and Ford won the day. It
was also attempted to be shown that the claim was on the
ground of the Hampton Plains Syndicate, who had ex-
clusive mining privileges over a large area. When an
offer, therefore, of £6OOO cash and a sixth interest was
made to Bayley and his mate it is no wonder that they
closed. From the Reward claim the present company in
the first six months of its operations took 52,000 odd
ounces of gold (over £200,000 worth). It is generally
concluded from the name of the mine—Bayley’s Reward
—that Bayley received a substantial sum for the dis-
covery ; but it is not so. The W.A. Govt, refused the re-
ward, only allowing the party as a recompense to each peg
two men's ground.
The latest Referendum vote in Switzer-
land has given a decided majority in favor of
State railway ownership.
Demise : George Anderson, ex-deputy-
master of Melbourne Mint. Mr. Anderson sat
for Glasgow in the Commons from 18G8 to 1885 as
an “ advanced ” Liberal. To Australians, English
Liberalism, even of the advanced school, does not
seem to advance very far. Mr. Anderson was
a believer in the limited-monarchial system, but
was one of those who opposed Disraeli’s jingoism.
For instance, he objected strenuously to the Dizzy
title of “ Empress of India ” for Queen Victoria,
on the ground that “ Queen ” is a democratic title,
while “ Empress ” smacks of autocracy. For his
political services he was, in 1885, appointed by
Gladstone to the charge of Melb. Mint, the
deputy-masterships at Sydney and Melbourne
being the only Australasian positions now in the
gift of the English Government, excluding Gover-
norships. He succeeded to Deputy-Master Delves
Broughton, an erratic and eccentric official from
the London Treasury, who, among other foibles,
conceived the notion of buying 1000 horses as a
present for the Prince of Wales ; to see the
heir to the throne, this official conceived,
driving 1000 beautiful horses down the Strand
would have been a world-enchanting sight.
It became necessary to put Mr. Broughton under
restraint, and his death soon occurred. Mr. Ander-
son (he could have dubbed himself “Major” on
his visiting cards had he been disposed to vaunt
his honors as an amateur soldier) was a man of
splendid physique and appearance, and was un-
doubtedly one of the handsomest men ever in the
Australian Civil Service. He was white-haired
and white-bearded, but no one would have
suspected that his birth dated back to 1819. He
was educated at Edinburgh High School and St.
Andrews University, and was for many years a
merchant in Glasgow. He married in 77, and is
survived by Mrs. Anderson and two children. He
has published many pamphlets on wages, educa-
tion and currency—and a few years ago gave evi-
dence before a Vic. Royal Commission in favor of
10-shilling and 5-shilling bank-notes. It is not
long since he published “ The Agnostic and Other
Poems ” —being the collected verses of his life-
time. He avowed that “The Agnostic” was
written to induce his readers to think out for
themselves the important concerns of Life, Death
and Eternity without taking beliefs second-hand
from parson, priest or presbyter. One of the
characters in the name-poem declares that the
priests cling
to the present world,
Its power, its pomp, its wealth, as if at heart
They held some lingering doubts of one to come.


A newly-invented Yankee drink, which
seems to be the perfection of moist joy, costs five
dollars a time. Four sprigs of mint and three
lumps of sugar are crushed in a dessert-spoonful of
water, and a wine-glass of brandy is added. Next
a pint of champagne. Then two table-spoonfuls
of crushed ice, and slices of pineapple, banana and
orange. Next comes a topping of ice-cream orna-
mented with a few strawberries and raspberries
and three or four rosebuds.
In N.S.W. last year one out of every 343
inhabitants was a lunatic—a known lunatic, that
is, for there is always a fringe of insanity in the
regions Out Back among the lonesome shepherds
and bushmen, which doesn’t come under official
notice until the patient goes into the town to
drink his cheque and tries to set fire to the grog-
shanty. In 1886, which was the best year in the
last 20, there was only one crank to every 379
people. Lunacy gradually grew more prevalent
from 76 to ’B2, then it diminished in an irregular
way till ’B6 ; since then it has increased, and ’95
was the worst year in the records of the province.
Still, lunacy is not nearly so common in N.S.W.
as in the United Kingdom—particularly in Ire-
land which has a woefully bad record. “Worry,”
either through bereavement, bank reconstruction,
income-tax, overwork, or other causes, is said to
have created 93 of the lunatics now on hand ; the
accursed drink comes next with 51, and epilepsy is
a good third with 49.
